الفصل (٤٥) انحلال العقد
حق الفسخ لأحد العاقدين عند تخلف العاقد الآخر عن تنفيذ التزامه في حينه إن لم تقبله بعض المذاهب ففي سواها وفي قواعد الشريعة الإسلامية متسع له .
Chapter Six: The Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. A – From the perspective of juristic principles and foundations, the prophetic hadith: 'No harm and no reciprocating harm' (references) supports the right of rescission. For executing the contract at a time other than the agreed time due to the default or defiance of one of the contracting parties may cause great harm to the other party, and harm is negated by the text, and the judiciary cannot turn back time. B – In the schools, the Hanbali jurists explicitly state in the contract of marriage that if a woman stipulates a valid condition on the man in the marriage contract and he does not fulfill it, she has the right to rescind the marriage. The scholar Muwaffaq al-Din Abdullah ibn Qudamah al-Maqdisi said in the chapter on conditions in marriage in his book 'al-Muqni': 'Conditions are of two types: a valid condition, such as stipulating an increase in dower, or a specific currency, or that he not take her out of her house or country, or that he not marry another; this is valid and binding. If he fulfills it, otherwise she has the right to rescind.' You see that most of these conditions the judge can force the husband to respect and implement, yet the woman was given the right of rescission. If granting her the right of rescission for this reason is valid in marriage, which is the least dissoluble of contracts – and thus cancellation (iqala) is not applicable – then in other than marriage, purely financial contracts, fiqh accepts this right by priority. Also in the seventh option chapter of the book of sales in 'al-Muqni' by Ibn Qudamah himself, it says: 'If the price is a debt, the seller is compelled to deliver the sold item, then the buyer is compelled to deliver the price if it is present. If the price is far away, or the buyer is insolvent, then the seller has the right to rescind.' etc. There are also texts in the Maliki school that show, for the investigator, that granting the right of rescission to one of the contracting parties when the other party fails to fulfill his obligation at its time, if not accepted by some schools, is accommodated in others and in the principles of Islamic law.
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage argues for the right of rescission (al-faskh) when one party fails to perform its obligation, causing harm to the other. It begins by citing the major qawida 'la darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no reciprocating harm), a foundational maxim rooted in a prophetic hadith. The author explains that non-performance at the agreed time constitutes harm, and since harm is negated by the text, the law must provide a remedy; the only effective remedy is rescission because time cannot be reversed. The passage then supports this by presenting examples from the Hanbali school. First, in marriage: if a wife stipulates a valid condition (e.g., that her husband not take another wife) and he fails to fulfill it, she has the right to rescind the marriage. The author quotes Ibn Qudamah's al-Muqni to confirm this. The author stresses that most such conditions can be enforced by the judge, yet the law still grants rescission as a right. This is significant because marriage is the least dissoluble of contracts (it cannot be voided by mutual cancellation, iqala), so if rescission is allowed there, it should be even more applicable in purely financial contracts. Second, in sales: if the buyer is insolvent or the price is distant, the seller may rescind. The passage also hints at Maliki support, concluding that even if some schools do not accept this right, others and the general principles of Sharia do. The overall argument is that the right of rescission for non-performance is a sound legal remedy grounded in both textual evidence (hadith) and juristic reasoning (qiyas awla).

Key Terms
حق الفسخ (haqq al-faskh): The legal right of a contracting party to terminate the contract unilaterally due to the other party's failure to perform a condition or obligation, as a remedy for harm.
لا ضرر ولا ضرار (la darar wa la dirar): A foundational legal maxim meaning that harming others is prohibited, and reciprocating harm is also prohibited. It is used to justify prevention and removal of harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is the primary textual basis for the right of rescission?
A) A Quranic verse on fulfilling contracts
B) The hadith 'No harm and no reciprocating harm'
C) Ijma of the Companions
D) Analogy with divorce

Key Takeaway
The qawida 'la darar wa la dirar' serves as a foundational source for the right of rescission across contracts, but its application must be calibrated against each school's specific rules.

Conditions for Hudud and the Shubuhat Principle
Lists conditions for theft hadd and applies shubuhat principle to four case types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions that trigger theft hadd and apply the qawida 'al-hudud tudra'u bil-shubuhat' to evaluate when shubuhat prevent hadd punishment.

Core Idea
Hudud are averted by plausible doubts — certainty required for punishment.

This lesson examines the general conditions for applying hudud punishments in Hanafi law, with theft as a key example. It then introduces a foundational legal maxim: hudud are averted by shubuhat (doubts). This principle balances strict liability with mercy, ensuring that punishments are only applied when certainty is achieved. Understanding the conditions and the shubuhat maxim is essential for any mufti dealing with criminal cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions for theft hadd
From the passage, theft hadd requires: (1) the stolen item reaches nisab, (2) it is taken secretly, and (3) it is taken from a secure place (hirz). List these conditions.
These are specific to theft; other hudud have their own conditions.
2
Exclude cases that do not meet the conditions
The text explicitly excludes ghasb (usurpation), open taking, and denial of a deposit/loan because they lack secrecy or hirz. Understand why each fails.
These exclusions show the importance of each condition.
3
State the four examples of shubuhat
The passage gives four cases where shubha averts hadd: ignorance of prohibition, creditor taking from debtor, spouses stealing from each other, and mahram relatives stealing from each other. List each and identify the type of doubt.
Each shubha creates uncertainty in the conditions (e.g., hirz or ownership).
4
Apply the qawida to each example
For each shubha case, explain why the doubt is plausible and how it prevents the required certainty for hadd. Connect directly to the maxim 'al-hudud tudra'u bil-shubuhat'.
The doubt must be substantive; a mere claim is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣/٤٩ - شرائط الجرائم الموجبة للحدود : والجرائم الموجبة للحدود يشترط في صفة كل منها وكيفية وقوعها لكي يجب بها الحد شرائط عديدة اختلفت فيها الاجتهادات. وهي في مواطنها من كتب الفقه : فحد السرقة مثلاً من جملة شرائطه أن يكون المسروق نصاباً معيناً من المال، وأن يُؤخذ خفية من حرز حريز. فيخرج عنه الغَضب، والأخذُ جهراً، وجحد الوديعة أو العارية. ٤/٤٩ - تأثير الشبهات في درء الحدود: ويشترط أيضاً في الحدود جميعاً بوجه عام أن لا يكون لمرتكب جريمتها شبهة تجعل له شبه معذرة أو تقتضي تخفيف مسؤوليته، كما لو زنى أو سرق أو شرب الخمر جاهلاً بالتحريم، أو سرق الدائن من مدينه ما يعادل دينه ولو كان الدين مؤجّلاً ، أو سرق أحد الزوجين من الآخر؛ أو سرَقَ الشخص من أحد أقاربه المحارم، لأن الخلطة بينهم عادة، ودخول بعضهم بيوت بعض يجعلان شبهة في تحقق شريطة الحرز تدرأ الحد .
3/49 - Conditions for crimes that necessitate hadd punishments: The crimes that necessitate hadd punishments require, in terms of their description and manner of occurrence in order for the hadd to be obligatory, many conditions in which ijtihads differ. They are in their places in books of fiqh. For example, among the conditions for the hadd of theft is that the stolen item be a specific nisab of wealth, and that it be taken secretly from a secure place (hirz). Thus excluded are usurpation (ghasb), open taking, and denial of a deposit or loan. 4/49 - Effect of shubuhat (doubts) in averting hadd punishments: It is also required generally in all hudud that the perpetrator of the crime not have a shubha (doubt) that gives him an excuse or mitigates his responsibility, such as if he committed zina, theft, or drank alcohol while ignorant of its prohibition, or if a creditor stole from his debtor an amount equal to the debt even if the debt is deferred, or if one spouse stole from the other, or if a person stole from one of his close relatives (mahram), because mixing between them is customary and their entering each other's houses creates a doubt regarding the fulfillment of the condition of hirz, which averts the hadd.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage outlines the general conditions for applying hudud and the crucial principle that shubuhat (doubts) avert the hadd punishment. For theft specifically, the conditions include a minimum nisab, secret taking from a secure place (hirz). Then the text introduces the rule of shubuhat: any doubt that provides an excuse or mitigates responsibility will prevent the hadd. Examples include ignorance of prohibition, a creditor taking from a debtor, spouses taking from each other, and relatives taking from each other due to customary access and shared spaces. This is a manifestation of the well-known qawida 'al-hudud tudra'u bil-shubuhat' (hudud are averted by doubts). The rationale is that the strictness of hudud requires certainty; doubts create uncertainty and thus the hadd is not applied.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A plausible doubt or ambiguity that gives the perpetrator a legal excuse or reduces responsibility, thus averting the hadd punishment.
حرز (hirz): A secure place or means of protection suitable for the type of property, such as a locked house or guarded area.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum quantity or value of stolen property required to trigger the hadd punishment for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the debtor gave permission tacitly
D) Because the value is less than the nisab

Key Takeaway
Hudud punishments require near-certainty; any plausible doubt (shubha) in the evidence or circumstances averts the hadd, based on the qawida 'al-hudud tudra'u bil-shubuhat'.

Distinguishing Nullity from Dissolution: A Foundational Qāʿida
Defining the difference between nullity and dissolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between nullity (buṭlān) and dissolution (infisākh) in Hanafi contract theory and apply the distinction to identify whether a contract is void ab initio or valid but subsequently dissolved.

Core Idea
Nullity invalidates a contract from its origin; dissolution terminates a valid contract due to supervening events.

In Hanafi legal theory, not all contract failures are equal. A contract may be void from inception due to a fundamental violation, or it may have been valid but later becomes impossible to perform. This passage clarifies the distinction between buṭlān (nullity) and infisākh (dissolution)—a distinction with significant practical consequences for rights, obligations, and remedies. Understand this well: it is a cornerstone of Hanafi contract analysis.

Guided Path
1
Define the two terms
Locate the definitions of buṭlān (nullity) and infisākh (dissolution) in the passage. Note the key difference: nullity arises from a hidden or overt fundamental violation at the time of contracting; dissolution arises after a valid contract due to a supervening event.
The author says nullity is 'مخالفة لنظام التشريع' (violation of legislative system) whereas dissolution is 'سبب طارئ' (a supervening cause).
2
Identify the temporal dimension
Nullity is retroactive: the contract is considered never to have existed. Dissolution is prospective: the contract was valid until the event made performance impossible.
The text says nullity makes the contract 'معدوم اعتباراً من أصله' (legally nonexistent from its origin), while dissolution occurs 'بعد تمام الانعقاد الصحيح' (after correct completion).
3
Analyze the examples
The passage gives examples: sale of destroyed goods, lease of destroyed property, partnership with destroyed capital. All illustrate dissolution (infisākh) after valid formation. Contrast with nullity examples you may know (e.g., contract without offer or acceptance).
These examples show that the supervening event makes performance impossible, not that the contract itself was defective.
4
Apply the qāʿida
Formulate the principle: 'Contracts are valid unless a fundamental violation exists at inception; after valid formation, they dissolve only upon a supervening impossibility.' This qāʿida governs both contract enforceability and remedy.
This is a wustā (intermediate) maxim within the broader theory of contracts.
5
Check for ikhtilāf
The passage notes that other schools may not distinguish as clearly between the two. Identify the practical consequence (thamarah): for nullity, no rights ever accrued; for dissolution, rights existed until the event, affecting claims for damages or restitution.
This distinction matters for responsibility: in dissolution, the contract was valid, so parties may have obligations (e.g., return of price) that differ from nullity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤/٥٢ - الفرق بين بطلان العقد وانفساخه : إنَّ الانفساخ الذي تقدم بيانه في بحث انحلال العقد آخر نظرية العقود هو شبيه بالبطلان. وقد يعبر فقهاؤنا عنه أحياناً بالبطلان تسامحاً في التعبير لكن الواقع أن بین البطلان بالمعنى والسبب اللذين سلف إيضاحهما ، وبين انفساخ العقود، فرقاً بيناً.
أ - فبطلان العقد يكون عن مخالفة لنظام التشريع من ناحية أساسية في عملية العقد. فالعقد عندئذ يكون من أصله باطلاً، أي معدوماً اعتباراً .. فإذا انعقد العقد على وجهه الصحيح لا يتصور بعد ذلك فيه مخالفة في عملية العقد لأنها قد انقضت إلا أن يتبين أنه قد كان هناك مخالفة مستورة توجب البطلان، فعندئذ يسترد الاعتبار فيعتبر باطلاً منذ نشأ. وبهذا يتجلى في بطلان العقد كونه مؤيّداً مدنياً لنظام التعاقد كما سلف بيانه .
فأما الانفساخ فإنما يكون بعد تمام الانعقاد الصحيح لسبب طارىء يمتنع معه بقاء العقد بعد وجوده كهلاك المبيع عند البائع بعد العقد قبل التسليم. فالعقد هنا لا مخالفة فيه، وهلاك المبيع بعد ذلك ليس مخالفة لنظام التعاقد، ولكنه حادث يتعذر معه تنفيذ العقد فينفسخ ومثل ذلك يلحظ في الإجارة إذا هلك المأجور وفي الشركة إذا هلك رأس المال المخصص لها.
4/52 - The difference between nullity of a contract and its dissolution: The dissolution (infisākh) previously explained in the discussion of contract termination at the end of the theory of contracts is similar to nullity. Sometimes our jurists loosely refer to it as nullity, but in reality there is a clear difference between nullity in the sense and cause that have been explained, and the dissolution of contracts. (a) Nullity of a contract arises from a violation of the legislative system in a fundamental aspect of the contracting process. The contract is then void from its origin, i.e., legally nonexistent. If the contract is concluded correctly, no violation in the contracting process can be conceived afterwards because it has concluded, unless an hidden violation becomes apparent that necessitates nullity, whereupon its legal consideration is revoked and it is considered void from inception. Thus nullity of a contract manifests itself as a civil sanction for the contracting system as previously explained. (b) As for dissolution, it occurs after the correct completion of the contract due to a supervening cause that prevents the continuation of the contract after its existence, such as the destruction of the sold item while in the seller's possession after the contract but before delivery. Here there is no violation in the contract; the subsequent destruction of the sold item is not a violation of the contracting system, but an event that renders the contract impossible to execute, so it dissolves. Similar is observed in lease if the leased property is destroyed, and in partnership if the capital allocated to it is destroyed.
Source pages p. 672

Commentary
This passage provides a critical conceptual distinction between batalan (nullity) and infisakh (dissolution) in Hanafi contract law. Nullity is an inherent defect: the contract was invalid from the start due to a fundamental violation of legal norms—it is as if the contract never existed. Dissolution, by contrast, applies to a contract that was correctly and validly formed but then becomes impossible to perform due to an external event (e.g., destruction of the subject matter). The author emphasizes that nullity is a sanction protecting the integrity of the contract system, while dissolution is a pragmatic response to supervening impossibility. Examples from sale, lease, and partnership illustrate the point. The note that jurists sometimes loosely call dissolution 'nullity' is a caution against terminological confusion.

Key Terms
بطلان (buṭlān): Nullity: the contract is void ab initio due to a fundamental legal violation present at the time of formation.
انفساخ (infisākh): Dissolution: termination of a valid contract due to a supervening event making performance impossible.
طارئ (ṭāri’): Supervening: occurring after the contract is concluded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes dissolution (infisākh)?
A) A contract that is void from the start due to a fundamental violation.
B) A contract that was valid but ends because of a later event making performance impossible.
C) A contract that is voidable at the option of one party.
D) A contract that is rescinded by mutual agreement.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction: nullity is a defect in the contract's formation; dissolution is an external event after valid formation.

Transformation of a Void Contract (Inqilab al-Aqd) — Refining the Hanafi View
Critical refinement of a Hanafi rule on contract transformation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa applying the maxim 'al-'ibra fi al-'uqud lil-maqasid' to determine whether a void sale can be recognized as a gift.

Core Idea
A void contract may transform into a valid one if its purpose is fulfilled.

This passage tackles a nuanced problem: when a sale is void because the price is explicitly negated, can it be salvaged as a gift? The preponderant Hanafi view says no, but the author argues for yes using the principle that contracts are judged by their purposes. As a mufti, you must navigate conflicting qawa'id and understand when transformation is possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial contract and its defect
Determine if the contract is a sale or ijarah with explicit negation of price or rent. This triggers the analysis of transformation possibilities.
2
Apply the maxim of purposes
Examine whether the contract contains elements of a different valid contract (gift or loan) that align with the parties' actual intention. Use the maxim 'al-'ibra fi al-'uqud lil-maqasid'.
3
Consider the counter-maxim
Evaluate the preponderant Hanafi view that void contracts are nonexistent and cannot transform. Weigh this against the rules: employing speech is preferable (إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله) and metaphor in contracts is accepted.
4
Weigh the evidence and tarjih
The author argues that the first view (transformation) is stronger, citing the qawa'id and the precedent of marriage by the word of sale. However, the preponderant Hanafi position opposes transformation. The mufti must decide which view to follow.
5
Formulate the fatwa
State the ruling: under the author's view, a void sale with no price can be treated as a gift if the elements (e.g., delivery) exist. Provide conditions and caveats.

الحالة الثالثة : في انقلاب العقد :
ومما يتصل بالآثار الاستثنائية للعقد الباطل ما يكون من انقلاب هذا العقد أحياناً إلى عقد آخر صحيح في بعض الأنظار الفقهية . فإن العقد الذي يُعتبر باطلاً في موضوعه الخاص قد يتضمن عناصر عقد آخر لا يتنافى مع غرض العاقدين، فينصرف إليه، وإن اعتبر باطلاً في موضوعه المتعاقد عليه .
مثال ذلك : أنَّ فقهاءنا حكموا ببطلان البيع والإجارة إذا صرح في عقدهما بنفي الثمن والأجرة صراحة بأن قال العاقد : بعتك هذا الشيء بلا ثمن، أو قال آجرتك إياه بلا أجرة وذلك لأنهما من عقود المعاوضة التي تقوم على أساس بدلين متقابلين. فإذا نفي الثمن صراحة انتفت حقيقة العقد وهي المعاوضة (٢).
ثم اختلفوا بعد تقرير بطلان البيع والإجارة :

- هل ينقلب العقد فينعقد البيع هبة، والإجارة إعارة؟
- وهل ينقلب البيع إعارة أيضاً إذا كان فيه توقيت مع نفي البدل، كما لو قال بعتك منفعة هذا الشيء شهراً بلا ثمن أو لا ينقلب العقد في شيء من ذلك؟
ذهب فريق من الفقهاء إلى هذا الانقلاب تنزيلاً على القاعدة العامة القائلة : العبرة في العقود للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ والمباني (ر: ف [؟])
وذهب آخرون إلى عدم هذا الانقلاب بحجة أن العقد الأصلي هنا باطل فهو معدوم والانقلاب لا يكون في معدوم. وهذا هو الرأي الظاهر الترجيح في الاجتهاد الحنفي ( ر [؟] رد المحتار ج ٤ و ٥ أول كتابي العارية
والإجارة).
ولكننا نرى أنَّ الرأي الأول ،أوجه والقواعد الفقهية تؤيده، فإن من القواعد المقررة أن إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله ما أمكن (المجلة/ (٦٠)
ومن صور إعمال الكلام حمله على المجاز إذا تعذرت الحقيقة (المجلة (٦١). فيعتبر التعبير بالبيع والإجارة مجازاً عن الهبة والإعارة. وإن المجاز في العقود معهود حتى لقد صرّح الفقهاء في النكاح على خطورة موضوعه أنه ينعقد بلفظ البيع على سبيل المجاز في التعبير (ر: الهداية وشروحها ۱۰۷/۳) (١).
(١) والنظر القانوني الحديث في الفقه الأجنبي يتجه إلى هذا فيما يسمونه: نظرية تحول وهي نظرية حديثة في الفقه الألماني. حتى إن أصحابها يتوسعون فيها فيقولون بانقلاب البيع إلى هبة ولو كان الثمن مذكوراً في العقد إذا كان الثمن تافهاً يسيراً بالنسبة إلى المبيع بحيث يدل على قصد التبرع. ر: الموجز في الالتزامات للأستاذ السنهوري، ف/ ١٦٨ / الحاشية، ونظرية العقد» له أيضاً، ف/ ٥٩٤/ وما بعدها). وبهذا أخذ القانون المدني السوري الذي صدر لدينا الآن وأصله القانون المصري الجديد. فقد صرحت المادة / ١٤٥/ منه بأنه :
Third case: Transformation of the contract. And among the exceptional effects of the void contract is that this contract sometimes transforms into another valid contract in some juristic views. For a contract that is considered void in its specific subject may contain elements of another contract that does not conflict with the purpose of the parties, so it is directed to it, even though it is considered void in its contracted subject. Example: Our jurists ruled the invalidity of sale and ijarah if their contracts expressly negate the price and rent by saying: I sold you this thing without a price, or I rented you it without rent, because they are commutative contracts based on two counter-values. If the price is expressly negated, the reality of the contract (exchange) is negated. Then they differed after establishing the invalidity of sale and ijarah: (2) Contrary to when the price or rent is omitted without express negation, as if he said: I sold you this thing or I rented it to you, and the other accepted. Then the price or rent is considered, but it is unknown due to absence of statement, so the contract is invalid (fasid), as will be seen later. Chapter (53) Results of Invalidity: Does the contract transform so that the sale becomes a gift and the ijarah becomes a loan (i'arah)? And does the sale transform into a loan if there is a fixed term with negation of consideration, as if he said: I sold you the benefit of this thing for a month without price, or does the contract not transform in any of this? A group of jurists adopted this transformation based on the general maxim: Consideration in contracts is for purposes and meanings, not for words and forms (ref: [؟]). Others adopted non-transformation, arguing that the original contract here is void and thus nonexistent, and transformation cannot occur from nothing. This is the apparent preponderant view in Hanafi ijtihad (ref [؟] Radd al-Muhtar vol. 4 and 5 beginning of the books of 'ariyah and ijarah). But we see that the first view is more sound and the fiqh principles support it. For among the established rules is that employing speech is preferable to discarding it when possible (al-Majallah article 60), and among the methods of employing speech is interpreting it metaphorically if the literal is not possible (al-Majallah article 61). So the expression of sale and ijarah is considered metaphorical for gift and loan. And metaphor in contracts is well-known; indeed the jurists declared in marriage, despite its seriousness, that it may be concluded by the word of sale as a metaphorical expression (ref: al-Hidayah and its commentaries 3/107). (1) Modern legal theory in foreign jurisprudence tends to this in what they call the theory of transformation, a modern theory in German law. Indeed its proponents expand it, saying that a sale transforms into a gift even if the price is mentioned if the price is trivial and negligible relative to the sold item, indicating intent of gratuity. The Syrian Civil Code (originating from the new Egyptian Code) adopted this; article 145 states: ...
Source pages p. 677

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of transformation (inqilab) of a void contract. It is introduced as an exceptional effect of a void contract. The author presents the issue: if a sale or ijarah explicitly negates the price/rent, the contract is void. The question is whether such a void contract can transform into a valid gift (hiba) or loan (i'arah). Two views are presented: (1) Some scholars say yes, based on the maxim that contracts are judged by purposes, not words. (2) Other scholars (the apparent preponderant view in the Hanafi school) say no, because a void contract is nonexistent and cannot transform. The author then argues in favor of the first view, supporting it with the rules of preferring operation of words and the use of metaphor. He also cites modern legal theory as a parallel. This lesson is a critical refinement of the Hanafi position, where the author expresses his preference contrary to the preponderant view.

Key Terms
انقلاب العقد (inqilab al-aqd): The transformation of a void or invalid contract into a valid contract of a different type based on the parties' intention and the elements present.
العبرة في العقود للمقاصد (al-'ibra fi al-'uqud lil-maqasid): The criterion in contracts is their purposes and real meanings, not the literal expressions.
إعمال الكلام أولى من إهماله (i'mal al-kalam awla min ihmali): Employing speech is preferable to discarding it when possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main reason supporting the transformative view?
A) The contract is not truly void because the price is implied.
B) The maxim that contracts are judged by purposes and meanings, and the rules preferring operation of speech.
C) The void contract is valid if both parties agree to ignore the price.
D) The Hanafi school has always accepted transformation.

Key Takeaway
The transformation of void contracts is a contested issue in Hanafi law; the preponderant view rejects it, but a strong argument based on qawa'id supports limited transformation where the purpose aligns with a different contract type.

Void Contract Needs No Rescission and Cannot Be Used in Court
Explains why a void contract needs no rescission and its practical implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that a void contract (al-‘aqd al-bāṭil) requires no rescission and to explain its practical effect as a defensive claim in court.

Core Idea
A void contract is legally non-existent; it needs no rescission and cannot be used in court.

This lesson unpacks a foundational Hanafi maxim: a void contract (bāṭil) is treated as if it never existed. Unlike a valid contract that may require rescission (faskh) through option rights, a void contract produces no legal effects from the outset. The author illustrates this with examples from sale and marriage, and highlights a crucial exception: even a void marriage, if consummated, imposes the ‘iddah (waiting period) to protect lineage.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish faskh (rescission) from butlān (voidness)
Faskh applies to existing contracts with an option (khiyār); butlān means the contract never legally existed.
Faskh removes a valid binding; butlān is a declaration of non-existence.
2
Identify the legal nature of a void contract
A void contract is non-existent (ma‘dūm), so any attempt to rescind it would be a tautology (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil).
This is the core logic of the maxim.
3
Apply the rule to sale and marriage
In a void sale, the seller can resell the item; in a void marriage, the woman can remarry without needing a divorce.
Both examples show immediate, extrajudicial disregard.
4
Recognize the defensive claim (daf‘)
If the other party sues, the defendant can simply assert nullity; no need to file a counterclaim for rescission.
The burden of proof is on the one claiming validity.
5
Note the exception: consummation triggers iddah
Even in a void marriage, if consummated, the woman must observe iddah to avoid mixing lineages.
This exception protects a higher maqṣad (lineage).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعاً : العقد الباطل لا يحتاج إلى فسخ، ولا يحتج به أمام القضاء . أ - فأما كونه لا يحتاج إلى فسخ فلأن الفسخ إنما يحتاج إليه في عقد قائم يكون لأحد طرفيه الخيار في التحلل من رابطته أو بقائه . ثم قد يستقل الطرف المخيّر عندئذ في الفسخ كما في خيار الشرط في البيع، إذ يفسخه صاحب الخيار خلال المدة المشروطة له دون حاجة إلى رضاء أو قضاء وقد يحتاج الفسخ إلى رضاء أو قضاء، كما في خيار العيب. وعلى كل حال إذا لم يفسخ العقد المستحق للفسخ يبقى ساري الحكم نافذ المفعول. أما العقد الباطل فهو معدوم لم يوجد؛ وإزالة المعدوم تحصيل للحاصل، وهذا عند علماء المعقول مستحيل . فلكل من العاقدين أن يعتبر العقد الباطل معدوماً، ويتصرف بناء على هذا الاعتبار : - فإذا كان العقد الباطل بيعاً مثلاً فللبائع بيع العين المبيعة ثانية أو استعمالها واستهلاكها لأنها لا تزال على ملكه - وإذا كان العقد الباطل زواجاً فللمرأة أن تتزوج زوجاً آخر . فإن أقام المشتري أو الزوج في العقد الباطل دعوى أمام القضاء فطالب المشتري بتسليم المبيع أو طالب الزوج المرأة بالمتابعة أمكن الطرف الآخر أن يدفع دعواه دفعا ببطلان العقد. وهكذا يستغني المتمسك بالبطلان عن تجشم إقامة الدعوى لإبطال العقد الباطل، ويكتفي شرعاً وقانوناً بموقف سلبي يكون به دافعاً لدعوى خصمه إذا هوجم بها. على أنه إذا كان من الناحية النظرية لا حاجة بمن يتمسك بالبطلان إلى إقامة الدعوى لأجل الإبطال فمن الناحية العملية قد يحتاج إلى إقامة (1) يلحظ هنا أنه إذا أعقب الزواج الباطل دخول فإنه تترتب على الدخول عدة على المرأة فلا يجوز لها الزواج حتى تنقضي عدتها، ليعرف فراغ الرحم منعاً لاختلاط الأنساب .
Fourth: A void contract does not need rescission, and it cannot be used as evidence before the court. A - As for not needing rescission, it is because rescission is only needed for an existing contract where one of the parties has the option to dissolve or keep it. Then the party with the option may rescind independently, as in the option of condition in sale, where the holder of the option rescinds during the stipulated period without need for consent or court ruling; and sometimes rescission may need consent or court ruling, as in the option of defect. In any case, if the contract subject to rescission is not rescinded, it remains in effect. As for a void contract, it is non-existent, never existed; removing something non-existent is a tautology, and this is considered impossible by the rational scholars. So each of the two contracting parties may consider the void contract as non-existent and act accordingly: if the void contract is a sale, for example, the seller may sell the sold item again or use and consume it, because it is still his property; and if the void contract is a marriage, the woman may marry another husband. If the buyer or the husband in a void contract files a lawsuit before the court, demanding delivery of the sold item or the wife to comply, the other party can defend by asserting the contract's nullity. Thus the person holding to nullity is spared the trouble of filing a suit to invalidate the void contract, and is content, legally and religiously, with a defensive posture to ward off the opponent's claim if attacked. However, if theoretically there is no need for the one holding to nullity to file a suit for invalidation, practically he may need to do so. (1) Note here that if the void marriage was followed by consummation, then due to consummation an iddah (waiting period) becomes obligatory upon the woman, so she may not marry until her iddah expires, to ascertain the emptiness of the womb and prevent mixing of lineages.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage defines the legal nature of a void contract (bāṭil) by explaining two consequences: it does not require rescission (faskh) and cannot be used in court. The author contrasts rescission, which applies to a valid contract with an option, with a void contract which is legally non-existent. Because it is non-existent, no act of rescission is needed – one simply ignores it. Practically, this means the seller can resell the property and the woman can remarry. If the opposing party sues, the other can defend by simply asserting nullity. However, a practical caveat is noted: sometimes it may be necessary to go to court, for example to establish the nullity officially. The footnote adds an important exception: if the void marriage was consummated, the woman must observe iddah before remarrying to avoid lineage confusion. This is a typical Hanafī emphasis on preventing harm (darar).

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or dissolution of a valid contract due to an option (khiyār).
عدة (‘iddah): A waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of spouse, or after consummation of a void marriage, before remarrying.
دفع (daf‘): A defensive claim; the party asserting nullity can simply defend against a lawsuit without filing a counterclaim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a void contract not require rescission according to the passage?
A) Because the contract is still valid until rescinded.
B) Because it is legally non-existent, so rescinding it would be a tautology.
C) Because the parties have mutual option to rescind.
D) Because only a judge can declare it void.

Key Takeaway
A void contract is legally non-existent; it requires no rescission and cannot be used as a basis for a lawsuit, except that consummation of a void marriage imposes an iddah obligation.

Definition of Gharar and Its First Type in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining gharar and beginning its classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of gharar, cite its proof from the Sunnah, and identify the first type of gharar that voids contracts.

Core Idea
Gharar in the very subject matter voids the contract absolutely.

This lesson introduces gharar—a key legal concept in Hanafi contract law. We will explore its linguistic meaning (deception), its technical definition (reliance on an illusory matter), and its basis in the Prophetic prohibition of 'bay' al-gharar'. The Hanafi school classifies gharar into two types; this lesson covers the first—gharar in the existence of the subject matter—which renders the contract void (batil). Examples include selling an unborn fetus or an undetermined catch.

Guided Path
1
Define gharar linguistically and technically
Begin with the Arabic root: al-gharar means deception (taghrir). Technically, it is a contract that relies on an unreliable, illusory matter, leading the party into a deceptive outcome.
Memorize: 'الغرر هو التعاقد على أمر موهوم غير موثوق'.
2
Identify the proof from the Sunnah
The prohibition is established by the hadith: 'The Prophet (ﷺ) prohibited the sale of gharar.' This is a dalil naqli (textual evidence) of the prohibition.
The exact wording of the hadith is not given here, but it is well-known in the collections of Muslim, Abu Dawood, etc.
3
Distinguish the two types of gharar (first type focus)
Hanafi fiqh divides gharar into (1) gharar in the very existence of the subject matter, which voids the contract, and (2) gharar in attributes or delivery, which may be tolerated if minor (to be discussed later).
The second type is not detailed in this passage due to page cut-off.
4
Apply the ruling to examples
Examples from the text: selling a fetus in the womb (uncertain if it exists) or selling the catch of a hunter (unknown if it will be caught). Both are void due to gharar in the subject matter.
The Hanafi position relies on the principle that uncertainty in the core of the contract invalidates it.

Source Text

والمراد به عند الفقهاء أن يعتمد التعاقد على أمر موهوم غير موثوق ..

Secondly: Gharar
4/55 - Al-Gharar (with two fathas) linguistically means deception, i.e., illusion and leading into danger (see: Al-Qamus).
What is meant by it according to jurists is that the contract relies on an illusory, unreliable matter.
It is called so because its outward appearance deceives the contracting party and leads him into an illusory result. Its basis is established in the Sunnah that the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, prohibited the sale of gharar (1).
Hanafi jurisprudence distinguishes in this regard between two types of gharar: First: gharar in the very subject matter of the contract, which necessitates the nullity of the contract, such as selling the fetus in its mother's womb, for it is invalid due to the possibility that it may be a swelling, or that it is born dead... and like the sale of the hunter's catch, and the diver's catch, i.e., what the hunter will bring out

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of gharar (uncertainty/risk) in contracts. It gives the linguistic meaning (deception) and the technical definition: a contract based on something illusory and unreliable. The prohibition is supported by the hadith 'Do not sell gharar.' The Hanafi school classifies gharar into two types: (1) gharar in the very existence of the subject matter, which renders the contract void (batil), e.g., selling an unborn animal or an undetermined catch. The text begins examples but cuts off. The Hanafi distinction is important: gharar in the substance invalidates absolutely; gharar in description or quantity may not invalidate if it is minor and tolerated by custom. This distinction is central to Hanafi contract law.

Key Terms
الغرر (al-gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a contract that involves deception and lack of knowledge
التغرير (al-taghrīr): Deception; the linguistic root of gharar

Key Takeaway
Gharar in the subject matter makes a contract void; this is a core Hanafi qawida with clear application to uncertain sales.

Classification of Contracts: Valid, Void, Voidable, Binding, Non-Binding
Classifies contracts into categories and defines their opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four binary classifications of contracts in Hanafi fiqh, state the precise opposite of each term, and apply this taxonomy to classify a given contract scenario.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, every contract falls into a precise legal state with its own opposite — misuse invalidates the ruling.

This passage presents a fundamental taxonomy of contract states in Hanafi fiqh. Understanding the distinction between mun'aqid and batil, sahih and fasid, nafidh and mutawaqqif, lazim and mukhayyar is crucial for any jurist or student. The author emphasizes that these terms have fixed opposites and must not be interchanged, as each state carries distinct legal effects. Mastering this classification is the first step in contract analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
The first split is between mun'aqid (concluded) and batil (void). A contract is mun'aqid if offer and acceptance have been exchanged; it is batil if a fundamental element is missing, e.g., no subject matter.
This binary is the starting point; if a contract is batil, no further classification is needed.
2
Subdivide concluded contracts
Once established as mun'aqid, a contract is either sahih (valid) or fasid (voidable). Sahih contracts meet all conditions; fasid contracts have a defect that can be cured (e.g., gharar in price).
Hanafi distinguishes fasid from batil; the contract is still considered concluded but defective.
3
Classify valid contracts as binding or suspended
A valid contract is either nafidh (binding/effective) or mutawaqqif (suspended). Nafidh means it takes effect immediately; mutawaqqif requires third-party consent (e.g., a minor's contract).
Suspended contracts are not void; they become binding upon ratification.
4
Distinguish between irrevocable and optional
A binding (nafidh) contract is either lazim (irrevocable) or mukhayyar (optional, with an option to rescind). The opposite of luzum is takhyir or 'adam al-luzum.
Non-binding here does not mean void; it means the party has the right to cancel.
5
Memorize the exact opposites
The author lists the precise pairs: in'iqad ↔ butlan; sihha ↔ fasad; nafadh ↔ tawaqquf; luzum ↔ takhyir/adam al-luzum. Never mix them.
This is a common source of error in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٥٦) نتيجة الفساد وما إليها
العقد : إما منعقد، وإما باطل.
والمنعقد : إما صحيح، وإما فاسد.
والصحيح: إما نافذ وإما متوقف(١) أو موقوف.
والنافذ: إما لازم وإما مخير، أو غير لازم.
(ر : المجلة / ١٠٥ - ١١٥ ، ورد المحتار ١٠٠/٤).
ويجب الانتباه إلى هذا التقابل بين أسماء هذه الأحوال وأسماء المؤيدات المقابلة لها في اصطلاح الفقهاء:
فالانعقاد يقابله : البطلان .
والصحة يقابلها : الفساد.
والنفاذ يقابله : التوقف .
واللزوم يقابله التخيير، أو عدم اللزوم.
ولا يجوز استعمال أحد هذه الألفاظ في غير موقعها، ولا مقابلتها بغير ما يقابلها لما يترتب على ذلك من خلط ،وإيهام، لاختلاف كل حالة عن الأخرى في المفهوم والأحكام
(١) العقد النافذ هو مالا يتوقف على إجازة غير العاقدين لعدم تعلق حق فيه لأحد غيرهما. والعقد المتوقف هو ما يحتاج إلى إجازة من غير العاقدين لمساسه بحقه، كما تقدم في بحث الآثار العامة للعقد في نظرية العقود. (ر: ف ٢/٣٨).
[؟]
Chapter (56): The Result of Invalidity and Related Matters
A contract is either concluded or void.
The concluded is either valid or voidable.
The valid is either binding or suspended (or contingent).
The binding is either irrevocable or optional, i.e. non-binding.
(Ref: al-Majalla / 105-115, Radd al-Muhtar 4/100).
Attention must be paid to this correspondence between the names of these states and the names of their opposites in the terminology of the jurists:
Concluded is opposed by voidness.
Validity is opposed by voidability.
Bindingness is opposed by suspension.
Irrevocability is opposed by option, or non-binding.
It is not permissible to use any of these terms in other than their proper place, nor to pair them with other than their proper opposites, because of the confusion and ambiguity that would result, as each state differs from the others in concept and rulings.
(1) The binding contract is that which does not depend on the permission of non-contracting parties because no right of anyone else is attached to it. The suspended contract is that which needs permission from non-contracting parties because it affects their right, as previously discussed in the general effects of contracts in contract theory. (Ref: F 2/38).
[؟]
Source pages p. 714

Commentary
This passage is a classification taxonomy of contracts in Hanafi fiqh. The author first divides every contract into either 'mun'aqid' (concluded) or 'bāṭil' (void). The concluded contract is further divided into 'ṣaḥīḥ' (valid) or 'fāsid' (voidable). Valid contracts are either 'nāfidh' (binding) or 'mutawaqqif/mawqūf' (suspended). The binding contract is either 'lāzim' (irrevocable) or 'mukhayyar' (optional, meaning non-binding). The author then lists the precise opposites: the opposite of in‘iqād (conclusion) is buṭlān (voidness); the opposite of ṣiḥḥa (validity) is fasād (voidability); the opposite of nafādh (bindingness) is tawaqquf (suspension); and the opposite of luzūm (irrevocability) is takhyīr or ‘adam al-luzūm (option or non-binding). The author warns against misusing these terms, as each has a distinct legal meaning and effect. This careful terminology is essential for proper fatwa and legal analysis.

Key Terms
الانعقاد (al-in'iqad): The state of being concluded; a contract that has been formed.
الصحة (al-sihha): Validity; a contract that fulfills all essential elements and conditions.
الفساد (al-fasad): Voidability; a contract with a defect that can be cured, or that has legal effect until annulled.
النفاذ (al-nafadh): Bindingness; a contract that is immediately effective without needing third-party consent.
اللزوم (al-luzum): Irrevocability; a contract that cannot be unilaterally rescinded.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, which is the correct opposite of 'nafādh' (bindingness)?
A) Buṭlān
B) Tawaqquf
C) Fasād
D) Takhyīr

Key Takeaway
Every contract state in Hanafi fiqh has a precise opposite; using the wrong pair invalidates the legal analysis.

Silence as Implied Permission: The Maxim of Al-Sukut
Legal ruling on guardian's silence as implied permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-sukut fi ma'rad al-hajat ila al-bayan bayan' to scenarios where silence indicates consent, and identify its scope and exceptions.

Core Idea
Silence speaks when speech is expected — the guardian's quiet consent makes the minor's trade valid.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational Hanafi legal maxim that treats silence as a form of expression in contexts where clarification is necessary. Derived from customary practice, this maxim is crucial for transactions involving minors. You will learn how the guardian's silence when witnessing his discerning minor engage in trade transforms the minor into an authorized party, preventing harm to those dealing with him in good faith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize the situation: a guardian (wali) sees his discerning minor child (mumayyiz) buying or selling with others, and remains silent.
2
Identify the legal problem
Without a rule, people would be misled by the guardian's apparent acceptance and think the minor is authorized, leading to invalid transactions.
3
State the maxim
The maxim is: 'al-sukut fi ma'rad al-hajat ila al-bayan bayan' — Silence in a context where clarification is needed is a declaration.
4
Apply the maxim
Since the guardian is expected to object if he does not permit, his silence is legally treated as a declaration of permission (idhn).
5
Note the conditions and exceptions
The maxim applies only when: (1) there is a need for clarification, (2) the silent person is capable of speaking, (3) there is no external factor preventing speech. It does not apply if silence is due to coercion, unawareness, or inability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٦٢) الطور الثالث للأهلية طور التمييز راه وليه يتعاطى مع الناس البيع والشراء فظل ساكتاً عنه، فإنه مأذوناً، يصبح وذلك لحاجة التعامل ودلالة الحال، إذ من المقرر في القواعد أن السكوت في معرض الحاجة إلى البيان بيان ( ر ) ف ۲۳۲ والمجلة / ٦٧) وإلا تغرّر الناس بدلالة حال الولي، وتورطوا في التعامل مع الصغير وهو غير مأذون.
Chapter (62): The third stage of legal capacity: the stage of discretion. If his guardian sees him dealing with people in buying and selling and remains silent about it, then he becomes authorized. This is due to the need for transactions and the indication of the situation, for it is established in the rules that silence in the context of need for clarification is a clarification. Otherwise, people would be deceived by the indication of the guardian's situation and would become entangled in dealing with the minor while he is not authorized.
Source pages p. 763

Commentary
This passage states a legal ruling regarding the authorization of a discerning minor. The passage type is a ruling explanation. It begins by establishing that if a guardian (wali) observes his discerning minor child engaging in buying and selling transactions with people and the guardian remains silent without objection, that silence constitutes implied permission (idhn). The reasoning is practical: because there is a need for transactions and the situation indicates consent. The author cites the well-established legal maxim: 'Silence in a context where clarification is needed is considered a declaration' (al-sukut fi ma'rad al-hajat ila al-bayan bayan). This maxim is used in Hanafi fiqh to infer consent from silence when there is a clear need for expression. The passage warns that without this ruling, people would be deceived by the guardian's apparent acquiescence and would deal with the minor believing he is authorized when he is not.

Key Terms
السكوت (al-sukut): Silence; in this context, the guardian's failure to speak against the minor's transactions.
المميز (al-mumayyiz): The discerning minor; a child who has reached the age of discretion (around 7 years) and can understand transactions to some extent.

D) Only after the guardian's death

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, silence can be legally binding as implied consent when the context demands speech.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Fifth Stage of Capacity: Rushd and Prohibition of Disposition
شرح أن منع تسليم المال قبل الرشد يستلزم منع التصرف

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'ma la yatimm al-wajib illa bihi fa huwa wajib' to derive that prohibiting the handover of wealth to a non-rashid minor implies prohibiting his disposition, and evaluate the implication for guardianship.

Core Idea
Prohibiting the handover of wealth to a minor before rushd necessarily prohibits his disposition, otherwise the prohibition would be futile.

In this lesson, we examine the fifth stage of legal capacity (rushd) in Hanafi fiqh. The Quranic verse (4:5) conditions the release of an orphan's wealth upon perceiving sound judgment, not merely reaching puberty. This passage demonstrates how the usul principle 'ma la yatimm al-wajib illa bihi fa huwa wajib' (that without which an obligation cannot be fulfilled is itself obligatory) is applied to derive the legal consequence: if the handover of wealth is prohibited, then the minor's own dispositions over that wealth must also be prohibited, otherwise the protection of his property would be meaningless.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on the non-rashid minor
The passage states that if a minor reaches puberty but rushd is not established, he remains deficient in capacity (qasir ahliyyat al-ada'), his wealth is withheld, and guardianship continues. Identify this as the base ruling.
This ruling applies to financial matters; personal guardianship (e.g., marriage) ends at puberty.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse (4:5) is cited: 'And test the orphans until they reach the age of marriage; then if you perceive in them sound judgment, release their property to them.' The operative phrase 'if you perceive in them sound judgment' makes rushd a condition.
The verse uses 'rushd' (sound judgment), not just bulugh (puberty).
3
Apply the usul principle to derive the implication
The author cites the principle 'ma la yatimm al-wajib illa bihi fa huwa wajib'. The obligation is to protect the minor's wealth from waste. That obligation cannot be fulfilled unless the minor is also prohibited from disposing of the wealth. Therefore, the prohibition of handover implies prohibition of disposition.
This is an application of the maxim to derive a necessary legal implication.
4
Examine the qawida and its furuat
The passage provides examples: command for wudu implies seeking water; obligation of jihad implies sufficient eating; returning usurped property implies bearing expense. Understand that the principle is general across fiqh.
The qawida is wusta (intermediate) rank, governing prerequisites of obligations.
5
Conclude the legal effect
Thus, the non-rashid minor's contracts and acknowledgments regarding his property are invalid (non-executed) until rushd is proven. This preserves the Legislator's purpose of protecting his wealth.
This is consistent with the rule that there is no futility in Shari'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٦٤) الطور الخامس للأهلية طور الرشد
وتنفذ تصرفاته وإقرارته، وتسلّم إليه أمواله (ر ) الأحوال الشخصية لقدري باشا م/ ٤٩٦) . وأما إذا لم يثبت رشده مع بلوغه فإنه يبقى قاصر أهلية الأداء، وتبقى أمواله ممنوعة عنه، وتستمر عليه الولاية من جهة المال(۱) حتى يثبت رشده . وأصل ذلك هو أن النص القرآني الذي سَلَفَ بيانه(٢) يعلق تسليم أموال اليتامى إليهم بظهور هذا الرشد ، ولا يكتفي بمجرد البلوغ. ٢/٦٤ - ولا شك أنَّ عدم جواز تسليم مال الشخص إليه قبل ظهور رشده المالي معناه بقاء الولاية المالية عليه وعدم نفاذ تصرفاته قبل ذلك، إذ لا فائدة لمنع أمواله عنه إذا كانت عقوده وإقراراته عليها نافذة، إذ يضيع عندئذ أمواله بأقواله وإن لم تصل إليها يده فلا يتحقق غرض الشارع في حمايته من سوء تصرفه (ر: م/ ۹۸۲).
ومن المقرر في علم أصول الفقه أنَّ ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب (۳). فالأمر التشريعي بشيء يعتبر أمراً أيضاً بما يستلزمه ذلك الشيء ولا يتم إلا به فالأمر بالوضوء للصلاة مثلاً هو أمر بالسعي إلى مكان الماء. وإيجاب الواجبات الشرعية التي تحتاج إلى قوة البدن، كالعبادات والجهاد، هو إيجاب للأكل والشرب الكافيين لحفظ القدرة على أداء هذه الواجبات. وكذا إيجاب رد المغصوب هو إيجاب لتحمل نفقة الرد إذا كان محتاجاً إلى نفقة، وهلم جراً...
(1) أما الولاية عليه من جهة النفس - وهي تتضمن سلطة التزويج الجبري والتأديب والتطبيب الجراحي، كما سيأتي في الفصل / ٦٦/ فإنها تنقطع عن الشخص بمجرد بلوغه عاقلاً وصيرورته مكلفاً. (۲) هو قوله تعالى في سورة النساء (٥/٤) : ( واتلوا البنى حتى إذا بلغوا النكاح فإن وانستم منهم رُشدًا فَادْفَعُوا إِلَيْهِمْ أَمْوَاله ). (۳) ر : أصول الفقه للخضري ص ٥٤ - ٥٦ ، والموافقات للشاطبي ٣٩٤/٢ طبعة المطبعة الرحمانية

فمن هذا المبدأ الأصولي يتضح أن المنع الشرعي من تسليم المال إلى صاحبه الناشيء قبل إيناس رشده يعتبر منعاً له من التصرف، وإلا لم يكن لمنع المال عنه من فائدة، بل قد يكون عبثاً. وقد صرح علماء الأصول أنه لا عبث في التشريع : الموافقات للشاطبي ١٥٨/١).
Chapter (64): The Fifth Stage of Capacity: The Stage of Maturity (Rushd). His dispositions and acknowledgments are valid, and his wealth is handed over to him (see: Personal Status of Qadri Pasha, art. 496). But if his rushd is not established upon his reaching puberty, then he remains deficient in capacity of performance, his wealth remains withheld from him, and guardianship over him continues with respect to property(1) until his rushd is proven. The basis for this is that the Quranic text that has been mentioned earlier(2) makes the handing over of the wealth of orphans to them contingent upon the manifestation of this rushd, and does not suffice with mere puberty. 2/64 - And there is no doubt that the impermissibility of handing over a person's wealth to him before the appearance of his financial rushd means the continuation of financial guardianship over him and the non-execution of his dispositions before that, because there is no benefit in preventing his wealth from him if his contracts and acknowledgments concerning it are valid, for then his wealth would be lost through his statements even if they do not reach his hand, and the Legislator's purpose of protecting him from mismanagement would not be achieved (see: Majallat al-Ahkam, art. 982).
And it is established in the science of Usul al-Fiqh that "that without which an obligation cannot be fulfilled is itself obligatory" (3). Thus, a legislative command for something is also a command for what that thing necessitates and cannot be fulfilled without, so the command for wudu for prayer, for example, is a command to go to the water source. And making obligatory the Shar'i obligations that require physical strength, such as acts of worship and jihad, is a making obligatory of sufficient eating and drinking to preserve the ability to perform these obligations. Likewise, making obligatory the return of a usurped item is a making obligatory of bearing the expense of return if it requires expense, and so on...
(1) As for guardianship over him with respect to his person - which includes the power of compulsory marriage, discipline, and surgical treatment, as will come in Chapter 66 - it is severed from the person upon his reaching puberty with sanity and becoming accountable. (2) It is His saying, Exalted, in Surat an-Nisa' (4:5): "And test the orphans until they reach the age of marriage; then if you perceive in them sound judgment, release their property to them." (3) Refer to: Usul al-Fiqh by al-Khidri, pp. 54-56, and al-Muwafaqat by al-Shatibi, vol. 2, p. 394, Rahmani Press edition.

From this usul principle it becomes clear that the legal prohibition of handing over wealth to its owner, the minor, before perceiving his rushd, is considered a prohibition of his disposition, otherwise there would be no benefit in preventing wealth from him, rather it would be futile. And the scholars of usul have stated that there is no futility in legislation: al-Muwafaqat by al-Shatibi, 1/158.
Source pages p. 771

Commentary
Passage type: Ruling on the fifth stage of legal capacity (rushd) and its implications. This passage establishes that if a person reaches puberty (bulugh) but his rushd is not yet proven, he remains under guardianship regarding property, and his property is not handed over to him. The evidence is the Quranic verse in Surah an-Nisa (4:5), which conditions the release of wealth to orphans upon 'perceiving in them sound judgment' (rushd), not merely reaching puberty. The author then reinforces this by citing the usul principle 'ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب' (that without which an obligation cannot be fulfilled is itself obligatory). Applying this: the obligation to prevent the minor from wasting wealth necessitates the prohibition of his disposition over it; otherwise, the prevention of handing over the property would be meaningless. The author illustrates the usul principle with examples: the command for wudu implies the command to seek water; obligation of worship requiring strength implies obligation of sufficient eating. Thus, the prohibition of handing over property implies prohibition of any disposal. Finally, he asserts that this is not futile legislation, as scholars of usul affirm that shari'a is free from futility.

Key Terms
الرشد (al-rushd): الرشد هو حسن التصرف في المال وصيانته من الإسراف والتبذير
الولاية (al-wilayah): الولاية هي السلطة الشرعية على شخص أو مال لرعايته وحفظه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal basis for deriving that prohibiting the handover of wealth to a non-rashid minor implies prohibiting his disposition?
A) The Quranic verse in Surah al-Nisa commands guardians to test orphans.
B) The maxim 'ma la yatimm al-wajib illa bihi fa huwa wajib'.
C) The consensus of the Sahaba on financial guardianship.
D) The principle that there is no futility in legislation.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of handing over wealth to a non-rashid minor legally necessitates prohibiting his disposition over that wealth, derived via the usul principle that the prerequisite of an obligation is itself obligatory.

The Authority of Custom (Sultan al-Urf) in Hanafi Law
Explaining the extensive authority of custom as a source of practical legal rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the authoritative role of custom (al-urf) in Islamic law and apply the qawida 'al-urf sultan al-urf' to practical fatwa scenarios, including recognizing its limits when custom contradicts nass.

Core Idea
Custom is a major source of practical rulings; it can generate, modify, and interpret legal norms.

Custom (al-urf) is not a minor consideration in Islamic law; it is a foundational source for many practical rulings. This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi explains the vast authority of custom, how it arises from evolving needs, and how it governs transactions, interpretation, and even criminal law. As a Hanafi mufti, understanding the scope and limits of custom is essential for issuing fatwas that respond to changing circumstances while remaining faithful to the Shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida and its rank
State the maxim: 'العرف في نظر الشريعة الإسلامية يعد مستنداً عظيم الشأن لكثير من الأحكام العملية'. Recognize that it is a major (kubra) universal (kulliya) qawida applicable to all fiqh chapters.
This qawida parallels 'العادة محكمة' but emphasizes the authoritative force of custom.
2
List the functions of custom
From the passage, custom can: (1) generate new rulings, (2) renew/modify existing ones, (3) make absolute or restrict, (4) reveal meanings in speech, (5) delineate rights and obligations.
These functions are all within the realm of practical rulings (mu'amalat, ta'zir), not ibadat unless specified.
3
Distinguish valid custom from invalid
Valid custom (urf sahih) must not contradict a definitive nass (Quran or mutawatir Sunnah). If it does, it is rejected (urf fasid). Also, custom must be consistent and widespread.
This is the dabt: 'Custom is only valid when it does not contradict a definitive nass.'
4
Apply to the furuat given
Take the examples: (a) ta'zir for insult – custom determines what is insulting; (b) ambiguous contract terms – custom clarifies meaning. For each, show how the qawida yields the ruling.
These are only two examples; the principle is broader.
5
Consider the istithna'at
Identify the exception: when custom directly contradicts a clear nass. Give an example (e.g., custom allowing riba). The governing maxim for ibadat is 'الأصل في العبادات التوقيف'.
This prevents misuse of custom to legalize the prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع والستون
سلطان العرف
١/٦٩ - إنَّ العرف في نظر الشريعة الإسلامية يعد مستنداً عظيم الشأن لكثير من الأحكام العملية بين الناس في شتى شعب الفقه وأبوابه، وله سلطان واسع المدى في توليد الأحكام وتجديدها، وتعديلها وتحديدها،
وإطلاقها وتقييدها. فالعرف تولده الحاجات المتجددة المتطورة، ثم يكون نظاماً حاكماً تدور به وعليه عجلة المعاملات بين الناس ويكشف عن معاني كلامهم و مراميه ويرسم حدود الحقوق والالتزامات وينير محجة القضاء.
ففي اعتبار العرف تسهيل كبير يغني عن كثير من النصوص التفصيلية في الأحكام التشريعية وفي عقود المعاملات، اعتماداً على ما هو معروف ومألوف في شتى الوقائع المحتملة.
٢/٦٩ - والعرف لا تغني عنه نصوص التشريع والتقنين، لأنها لا يمكن أن تستوعب جميع التفصيلات والاحتمالات، كما أن كثيراً من أحكامها الآمرة نفسها مبني على العرف ويتبدل فيه الحكم بتبدل العرف، فلا يمكن ترتيب حكم ثابت فيه .
وقلما يوجد باب من أبواب الفقه لا يكون للعرف مدخل في أحكامه،
حتى باب الجرائم والعقوبات.
ففي جريمة التعدي على الكرامة مثلاً بالشتم والإهانة إنما يعتبر من الكلام جرماً يستحق قائله العقوبة التعزيرية ما يكون في عرف الناس شتماً
وإهانة .
Chapter Sixty-Nine: The Authority of Custom. 1/69 - Indeed, custom in the view of Islamic law is considered a source of great importance for many practical rulings among people in various branches and chapters of jurisprudence, and it has vast authority in generating and renewing rulings, modifying and defining them, making them absolute or restricted. Custom is generated by evolving and renewed needs, then becomes a governing system around which the wheel of transactions among people revolves and by which it is oriented; it reveals the meanings and intentions of their speech, delineates the boundaries of rights and obligations, and illuminates the path of the judiciary. In considering custom there is great facilitation that dispenses with many detailed texts in legislative rulings and in contracts of transactions, relying on what is known and familiar in various possible incidents. 2/69 - And custom cannot be dispensed with by legislative and codified texts, because they cannot encompass all details and possibilities, just as many of their imperative rulings themselves are built upon custom, and the ruling changes with the change of custom, so a fixed ruling cannot be established therein. Rarely is there a chapter of fiqh in which custom does not have an entry into its rulings, even the chapter of crimes and punishments. For example, in the crime of assault on dignity such as insulting and humiliation, only that speech is considered a crime deserving ta'zir punishment which is considered insulting and humiliating in the custom of people.
Source pages p. 823

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa highlights the immense authority (sultan) of custom in Islamic law. Custom is not a minor consideration but a major source for practical rulings. It can generate new rulings, renew outdated ones, modify existing rules, and refine the scope of legal commands. Al-Zarqa emphasizes that custom itself arises from evolving needs, then becomes a governing system for transactions, helping to interpret parties' intentions and define rights. Recognising custom facilitates the law by reducing the need for exhaustive detailed texts. Legislative texts cannot cover every detail, and many imperative rulings are themselves founded on custom; thus, as custom changes, the ruling may change accordingly. Custom permeates nearly all fiqh chapters, even crimes and punishments – what constitutes insulting speech is determined by local custom. In Hanafi ifta', this principle is crucial: muftis must ascertain the prevailing custom when issuing fatwas in mu'amalat and ta'zir, and must be aware that fatwas may change with changing customs. This passage underscores the dynamism and flexibility of Islamic law through custom.

Key Terms
سلطان العرف (sultan al-urf): The authoritative force of custom in generating, modifying, and interpreting legal rulings.
العرف (al-urf): Recurring practices that are accepted by people as proper and become a reference for legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can legislative texts not dispense with custom?
A) Because custom is superior to revelation
B) Because custom changes over time and texts cannot cover all details
C) Because custom is the only source of law
D) Because criminal law is entirely based on custom

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) is an authoritative source for practical rulings in Hanafi law, capable of generating, modifying, and interpreting norms, but it must not contradict a definitive text (nass).

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Authority of Custom: Applications in Oaths, Contracts, and Maintenance
Demonstrating the legal effect of custom through multiple furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'custom is authoritative' to derive rulings in contracts and oaths, and identify its limitations based on the furuat provided.

Core Idea
Custom restricts literal meanings in oaths and fills gaps in contracts, but not when custom is unreliable.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm' demonstrates how the Hanafi school gives legal weight to custom through multiple practical examples. We will examine the maxim 'al-'adah muhakkamah' and see how it operates across oaths, agency, maintenance, hire, and even its limitation in agricultural leases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its authority
Locate the statement 'العادة محكمة' in the text and note that it is one of the major legal maxims (al-qawaid al-kubra). Understand that it means custom has authoritative force in shaping legal rulings.
2
Work through the oath examples
Apply the maxim to the oaths of not eating meat and not riding animals. Note how custom restricts the literal meaning to what is customary in the society.
For a city dweller, riding a camel in the city is not customary, so no breach.
3
Analyze agency and maintenance examples
See how custom defines the scope of an agent's authority and the amount of spousal maintenance. Custom determines the type of goods and the standard of living.
4
Examine hire and lease examples
In hiring a pack animal or leasing a house, custom governs the permissible use and capacity. Note the exception for agricultural land where custom is not sufficient.
Land lease requires explicit specification because crops vary in effect.
5
Synthesize the scope and limits
Summarize that custom applies to interpret general terms and fill gaps, but not where it would contradict explicit agreement or where custom is unreliable (as in land lease).
The exception (istithna) shows custom is not absolute.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ومقتضى ذلك أنه لو كان الحالف من أهل المدن في هذه العصور لا يحنث بركوب الجمل في المدينة، ويحنث بركوبه إذا كان مسافراً، كما يحنث الهندي دون العربي بركوب الفيل .

٢ - ولو وكل إنسان وكيلاً بشراء لحم أو خبز أو ثوب مثلاً تقيد عقد الوكالة بنوع اللحم أو الخبز المعتاد أكله وبالثوب المعتاد لبسه، فلو اشترى له نوعاً آخر غير معتاد لا يسري الشراء على الموكل بل يلتزم به الوكيل ( ر )) رسائل ابن عابدين (١١٥/٢).

(1) محكمة بتشديد الكاف المفتوحة، اسم مفعول من التحكيم.

الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
م / ۱۷۳ و ١٨١ و ١٨٤). وهنا نجد عرف الناس في طراز المعيشة ومستواها . قد فرض حدوداً شرعية الالتزام النفقة الذي أوجبه الشرع على الزوج.
٤ - وكذا لو استأجر دابة للحمل، فإنَّ له تحميلها النوع والقدر المعتاد مما لا ضرر منه عليها، فليس له أن يحملها أكثر من طاقتها المعتادة مطلقاً، ولا أن يحملها الأشياء الثقيلة كالحديد والأحجار إلا أن يأذن المؤجر. وعندئذ يتقيد من حيث المقدار أيضاً بطاقتها المعتادة؛ وإلا كان ضامناً للدابة إذا تلفت إذ يعتبر متعدياً (ر : المجلة / ٥٥٥/ وشروحها، ورد المحتار في الإجارة ٢٢/٥).
من اللازم في إجارة العقار تعيين نوع المنفعة المقصودة بصورة
تمكن من حسم النزاع.
هذا ومع يصح استئجار الدار والحانوت دون بيان ما استؤجرا لأجله . وعندئذ يصار إلى تحكيم العرف والعادة في كيفية استعماله وفي سواغية ربط الدواب فيه وليس له أن يمارس فيه ما يوهن أو يضر البناء كالحدادة والقصارة ونحوهما، لأن من الملحوظ منعه عرفاً إلا بإذن المؤجر (المجلة:
٥٢٧ و ٥٢٨).
فإذا لم يكن تعيين كيفية الاستعمال ممكناً بالعرف وجب بيانه بالعقد كما في استئجار الأرض للزراعة، فإنه يشترط في العقد بيان ما يراد زرعه فيها، أو التصريح بأن للمستأجر أن يزرع ما شاء؛ وذلك لاختلاف أنواع المزروعات في نفع الأرض وضررها (ر: المجلة / ٥٢٤) .
ولم يلجأ الفقهاء هنا إلى تحكيم العادة والعرف لأن الناس تزرع في الأرض شتى الأصناف القابلة لها، ولكن تتفاوت رغبات المؤجرين والأجور بالنسبة إلى أصناف المزروعات بحسب درجة تأثيرها في قوة الأرض
الإنباتية .
٦ - تدل الفروع الفقهية في أحكام الجوار أنه لا يجوز لأحدٍ ذي حق
Chapter (69): The Authority of Custom. If someone swears, 'I will not eat meat,' his oath refers to the meat customarily eaten among them, not other types. So if he eats beef, camel meat, or fish, he does not break his oath and no expiation is required, even though it is meat linguistically. Likewise, if he swears not to ride or not to ride an animal, he does not break his oath by riding any animal or by riding a human, even though a human is a type of animal; he only breaks his oath if he rides what is customarily ridden of beasts (Radd al-Muhtar, Book of Oaths, 3:90-91). Accordingly, if the one swearing is a city dweller in modern times, he does not break his oath by riding a camel in the city, but he breaks it if he rides while traveling, just as an Indian breaks his oath by riding an elephant but an Arab does not. The legal rationale for this and similar cases is that practical custom restricts the literal meaning of the word and directs the oath to customary practice, like verbal custom, based on the established rule that 'custom is authoritative' (Majalla article 36), i.e., in the view of the Law, custom has authority to which rulings of transactions are subject, so those rulings are established according to what custom dictates (Radd al-Muhtar 3:86 and 90-91). 2. If a person appoints an agent to buy meat, bread, or clothing, the agency contract is restricted to the type of meat or bread customarily eaten and the clothing customarily worn. If the agent buys another non-customary type, the purchase does not bind the principal; rather, the agent is liable (Rasa'il Ibn Abidin 2:115). 3. Spousal maintenance is obligatory upon the husband in the Hanafi school according to the customary amount for their peers and according to their wealth or poverty. If they are of middle status, maintenance is moderate; if wealthy, the maintenance of the wealthy; if poor, the maintenance of the poor (al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya fi al-Ahwal al-Shakhsiyya by Qadri Pasha al-Masri, articles 173, 181, 184). Here we see that people's custom in lifestyle and level has imposed Shar'i limits on the maintenance obligation that the Law imposed on the husband. 4. Likewise, if one hires a pack animal for carrying, he may load it with the type and amount customarily carried without harm to it. He may not load it beyond its customary capacity absolutely, nor load heavy items like iron and stones unless the lessor permits. Then it is also restricted in quantity to its customary capacity; otherwise he is liable for the animal if it perishes, as he is considered a transgressor (Majalla article 555 and commentaries, Radd al-Muhtar on Ijarah 5:22). In leasing real estate, it is necessary to specify the intended type of use in a way that resolves disputes. However, leasing a house or shop is valid without specifying the purpose for which they are rented. Then custom is referred to in determining its use and whether it is permissible to tie animals there. He may not engage in anything that weakens or damages the building, such as blacksmithing or laundering, because such is customarily prohibited unless the lessor permits (Majalla articles 527, 528). If the determination of use is not possible by custom, it must be specified in the contract, as in leasing land for agriculture, where it is required in the contract to state what is to be grown or to state that the lessee may grow whatever he wishes; this is due to the different types of crops in benefiting or harming the land (Majalla article 524). The jurists did not resort to custom here because people grow various crops suitable for the land, but the preferences of lessors and rents differ according to the degree of effect on the land's fertility. 6. The fiqh branches on the rulings of adjacency indicate that no one with a right
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This passage, from Mustafa al-Zarqa's 'al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm', is a qawida-with-furuat exposition demonstrating the legal authority of custom ('urf) in the Hanafi school. It opens by stating the maxim 'al-'adah muhakkamah' (custom is authoritative) and then illustrates it through six practical applications. The first application concerns oaths: when a person swears not to eat meat or not to ride an animal, the scope of the oath is determined by what is customarily eaten or ridden in that person's society, not by the literal linguistic meaning. Thus, if a city dweller swears not to ride an animal, he does not break his oath by riding a camel in the city, but does break it if he rides while traveling, because riding camels in cities is not customary. Similarly, an Indian would break his oath by riding an elephant (customary), but an Arab would not. The second application: in agency contracts, the agent's authority is limited by what is customary. If an agent buys a type of meat or clothing that is not customary for the principal, the principal is not bound. Third, spousal maintenance is determined by custom according to the couple's social status. Fourth, hiring a pack animal restricts loading to customary capacity and type. Fifth, leasing a house or shop without specifying use defaults to customary use, and the lessee cannot engage in harmful trades unless the lessor permits. However, for agricultural land, custom alone is insufficient to determine the crop because of varying effects on land, so specification in the contract is required. The passage consistently applies the principle that custom restricts literal meanings and fills gaps in contracts. All examples are from Hanafi fiqh sources such as Radd al-Muhtar, Majalla, and Ibn Abidin.

Key Terms
العادة (al-'adah): Custom or habit that is consistent and prevalent, which governs legal rulings in transactions and oaths
العرف (al-'urf): Customary practice, both verbal (in language) and practical (in actions), which is authoritative
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath; the scope of the oath is determined by custom, not literal meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle of 'custom is authoritative', if a person swears not to eat meat and then eats fish in a region where fish is not customarily considered meat for meals, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because fish is meat linguistically
B) No, because custom restricts the oath to the meat customarily eaten
C) Yes, because all types of meat are included
D) No, because fish is not meat at all

Key Takeaway
Custom is authoritative in Hanafi fiqh to restrict literal meanings and fill contractual gaps, but its application is limited where it is unreliable or contrary to explicit agreement.

Custom as Arbiter of Use and Liability in Hanafi Fiqh
Explains how custom sets limits in contracts, torts, and bailments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-urf muhakkam' to determine liability when a party exceeds customary use in contracts (ijarah, ariyah, wadi'ah) and torts, and identify the exception for agricultural leases.

Core Idea
Custom defines the limits of permissible use; exceeding it makes one liable, unless an explicit contract or istithna rules otherwise.

In Hanafi fiqh, custom (urf) serves as a subsidiary source that fills gaps in contracts and sets standards for reasonable behavior. This passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa illustrates how the maxim 'al-urf muhakkam' (custom is authoritative) operates across hire, lease, borrowing, deposit, and neighborly relations. By studying these examples, you will learn to identify when custom determines liability and when an explicit contractual term is required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract or situation
Read each example and determine the legal relationship: hiring a beast, leasing property, kindling fire as a neighbor, borrowing an item, or depositing goods.
2
Check the role of custom
In each case, ask: Does the contract specify the manner of use? If not, custom fills the gap. What is the customary use or capacity in that locale?
3
Determine if use exceeded custom
If the party used the item beyond what is customary (e.g., overloading, using for harmful activity, borrowing for an unusual purpose, entrusting to an atypical person), then that party becomes a transgressor (mut'addi) and is liable for damage or loss.
4
Identify the exception
Note that for agricultural leases, custom alone is insufficient because crops vary greatly in their effect on land. The contract must specify the crop. This is the only exception mentioned in the passage.
5
Apply the ruling
Use the above analysis to state the ruling: if conduct is within custom, no liability; if beyond, liability arises. This is the thamarah (practical consequence) of the maxim.
