ولم يلجأ الفقهاء هنا إلى تحكيم العادة والعرف لأن الناس تزرع في الأرض شتى الأصناف القابلة لها، ولكن تتفاوت رغبات المؤجرين والأجور بالنسبة إلى أصناف المزروعات بحسب درجة تأثيرها في قوة الأرض الإنباتية .
٦ - تدل الفروع الفقهية في أحكام الجوار أنه لا يجوز لأحدٍ ذي حق في منفعة أن يتجاوز في استيفاء حقه إلى حد يضر بغيره فلو فعل كان ضامناً للضرر.
ومقياس التجاوز وعدمه إنما هو العرف والعادة.
فلو أوقد صاحب الأرض أو مستأجرها ناراً في الأرض فتطاير منها ما أحرق شيئاً لجيرانه من بيادر أو غيرها؛ فإن كان أوقدها بصورة معتادة عرفاً فهو غير ضامن وإن تجاوز المعتاد أو أوقدها في هبوب الريح كان ضامناً (ر) الدرر شرح الغرر آخر الإجارة (٢٤٠/٢) (١)
٧ - وكذلك ليس للمستعير أن يجاوز المعتاد عرفاً في كيفية استعمال العارية. فإن فعل كان ضامناً إن هلكت أو تضررت، رغم أن العارية في الأصل تعتبر أمانة في يده غير مضمونة. وذلك لأنه بتجاوزه المعتاد في استعمالها يصبح في العرف متعدياً.
۸ - إن مقتضى عقد الإيداع أن يحفظ الوديع الوديعة بنفسه في حرز مثلها عرفاً، وأن لا يودعها عند غيره لأن مبنى الإيداع على الثقة الشخصية بالوديع نفسه .
على أن الفقهاء سوّغوا للوديع على سبيل الاستثناء أن يحفظ الوديعة بواسطة من في عياله من زوجة أو ولد كبير أو أجير خاص غير مياوم، لأن الوديع الاستعانة لا يستغني عن ، ولا يعتبر بذلك مقصراً في الحفظ عرفاً، فلا يضمن بهم، الوديعة إذا تلفت أو سرقت عندهم، لكن بشرط أن لا يحفظها إلا عند من يحفظ مثلها عند مثله عادة وعرفاً، فيحفظ مثلاً فرس الوديعة عند سائسه أو خادمه، ويحفظ عقد الجوهر عند زوجته . فلو حفظ العقد النفيس عند الخادم كان مفرطاً مقصراً عرفاً فيضمنه إن ضاع أو تلف (ر ): المجلة / ۷۸۰ - ۷۸۲ وشروحها).
(1) وهذا يتلاقى ويتفق مع ما استقر عليه التفكير القانوني اليوم في النظرية الحقوقية الحديثة المسماة: نظرية التعسف في استعمال الحق : abus de droit فالشريعة الإسلامية قد سبقت إلى تقرير موضوع هذه النظرية في شتى فروع الأحكام التي يعرفها المطلعون .
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. M/173, 181, 184. Here we find that the custom of people in lifestyle and standard has imposed legal limits on the obligation of maintenance that the law imposed on the husband. 4 - Similarly, if one hires a beast for carrying, he may load it with the type and amount that is customary, which does not cause harm to it. He may not load it beyond its customary capacity absolutely, nor may he load it with heavy items like iron and stones unless the lessor permits. In that case, he is also restricted in quantity by its customary capacity; otherwise he is liable for the beast if it perishes, as he is considered a transgressor. (See: Majalla 555 and its commentaries, Radd al-Muhtar on Ijarah 5/22). It is necessary in leasing real estate to specify the intended type of benefit in a manner that precludes dispute. Yet, it is valid to lease a house or shop without stating what they are leased for. In that case, custom and usage are referred to in determining the manner of use and the permissibility of tying animals therein. He may not engage in what weakens or damages the structure, such as blacksmithing, fulling, and the like, because it is considered prohibited customarily unless the lessor permits. (Majalla: 527, 528). If determining the manner of use is not possible by custom, it must be stated in the contract, as in leasing land for cultivation; it is required in the contract to state what is intended to be grown, or to explicitly allow the lessee to grow whatever he wishes, due to the variation of crops in their benefit or harm to the land. (See: Majalla 524). The jurists did not resort to custom here because people grow various suitable crops in the land, but the desires of lessors and rents vary with the crops according to their impact on the land's fertility. 6 - Juristic branches in the rules of neighborly relations indicate that no one with a right to a benefit may exceed in exercising his right to the extent of harming others; if he does, he is liable for the harm. The measure of transgression and non-transgression is custom and usage. So if the owner or lessee of land kindles a fire in the land and sparks from it burn something of the neighbors, such as stacks or other things: if he kindled it in a customarily usual manner, he is not liable; if he exceeded the customary manner or kindled it while the wind was blowing, he is liable. (See: al-Durar sharh al-Ghurar, end of Ijarah 2/240). 7 - Likewise, the borrower may not exceed what is customarily usual in the manner of using the borrowed item. If he does, he is liable if it perishes or is damaged, even though a loan is originally considered a trust in his hand, not guaranteed. This is because by exceeding what is customary, he becomes a transgressor in custom. 8 - The requirement of a contract of deposit is that the depositary keep the deposit himself in a place that is customary for its like, and not entrust it to others, because deposit is based on personal trust in the depositary himself. However, the jurists permitted the depositary, by way of exception, to keep the deposit through those in his household, such as his wife, an adult child, or a specific non-daily hired worker, because the depositary cannot dispense with assistance, and he is not considered negligent in preservation customarily. Thus he is not liable for the deposit if it perishes or is stolen with them, but on condition that he only entrusts it to one who customarily keeps its like. For example, he may keep a horse deposited with his groom or servant, and keep a necklace of jewels with his wife. If he keeps the precious necklace with the servant, he is negligent and remiss customarily, and is liable if it is lost or perishes. (See: Majalla 780-782 and their commentaries). (1) This coincides and agrees with what modern legal thought has settled upon in the modern legal theory called: The Theory of Abuse of Right (abus de droit). Islamic law preceded in establishing the subject of this theory in various branches of rulings known to experts.
Source pages p. 830

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the role of custom (urf) in Islamic jurisprudence, specifically how it determines the limits of permissible use and liability in various contracts. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, presents several examples from Hanafi fiqh to illustrate that custom serves as a normative standard for what is considered normal, permissible, or transgressive.

1. **Maintenance (Nafaqa):** Custom (standards of living) determines the amount of maintenance a husband must provide.
2. **Hiring a beast of burden:** The custom of the area dictates the normal load; exceeding it makes the hirer liable for damages. If the owner permits a heavier load, the hirer is still bound by the animal's usual capacity.
3. **Leasing property (house/shop):** If the contract does not specify the use, custom determines what is acceptable. Activities that weaken the structure (like blacksmithing) are prohibited unless the landlord permits. For agricultural land, because crops vary in their effect on soil, custom is insufficient; the contract must specify the crop.
4. **Neighborly relations (torts):** The measure of transgression in exercising one's right (e.g., lighting a fire) is custom. If done customarily, no liability; if unusual or in a strong wind, liable. This aligns with the modern 'abuse of right' theory.
5. **Loan (Ariyah):** The borrower must use the item customarily; exceeding that makes him liable even though a loan is normally a trust.
6. **Deposit (Wadi'ah):** The depositary must keep the deposit himself. Exception: he may delegate to his household (wife, adult child, specific employee) if that is customary. But he must entrust it to someone who normally would keep such items (e.g., a horse with a groom, jewelry with his wife). If he gives a precious necklace to a servant, he is negligent and liable.

The author emphasizes that custom is the arbiter of what is reasonable, and exceeding it results in liability. This principle is consistent across multiple areas of fiqh.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Customary practice of the people in a particular society that is not opposed to Shariah and serves as a source of law.
العادة (al-‘adah): Repeated practice that becomes normative, synonymous with urf in this context.
ضامن (dāmin): Liable for compensation; responsible for damages or loss.
مستأجر (musta’jir): Lessee or hirer.
مؤجر (mu’jir): Lessor or landlord.
وديع (wadī‘): Depositary; the person entrusted with a deposit.
عارية (‘āriyah): Loan of a non-fungible item for use, without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition does a depositary become liable for loss of a deposit when he delegates its keeping to someone else?
A) He is liable in all cases of delegation.
B) He is liable if he delegates to anyone outside his household.
C) He is liable if he entrusts the deposit to a person who does not customarily keep such items (e.g., giving a precious necklace to a servant).
D) He is never liable because delegation is always allowed.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) is a binding source in Hanafi fiqh that sets the standard for permissible use and liability; exceeding it makes one a guarantor (damin).

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
beginner

The Maxim: Rulings Based on Custom Change with Custom
Legal maxim definition and scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'الأحكام المبنية على العرف تتبدل بتبدله', identify its scope and exceptions, and apply it to a new furu' case in transactions.

Core Idea
Rulings built on custom are dynamic: when custom shifts, the ruling shifts.

This lesson introduces a pivotal legal maxim in Hanafi jurisprudence: 'الأحكام المبنية على العرف تتبدل بتبدله' (Rulings based on custom change with its change). This maxim encapsulates the dynamism of Hanafi fiqh, particularly in transactions and social dealings. Understanding this principle is essential for a mufti to issue context-sensitive fatwas while respecting fixed texts.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Maxim
Commit the exact Arabic to memory: 'الأحكام المبنية على العرف تتبدل بتبدله'. Its English equivalent is 'Rulings based on custom change with its change.'
2
Understand the Scope
This maxim applies only to rulings that are derived from or conditioned by custom ('urf). It does not apply to rulings grounded in definitive Quranic or Hadith texts (nusus qat'iyya).
The scope is primarily in muamalat (transactions), not ibadat (worship).
3
Identify the Exception
Rulings established by explicit texts (nass) are fixed and not subject to change by custom. The governing maxim for such rulings is 'لا مساغ للاجتهاد في مورد النص' (No room for ijtihad in the presence of a text).
This exception ensures that core obligations remain stable.
4
Apply to a Furu' Case
Given a scenario where a custom has changed, derive the new ruling. For example, the custom of using a specific currency for payment: if the community shifts to a new currency, the ruling on the mode of payment adjusts accordingly.
The key is identifying the 'illa (effective cause) as the custom.
5
Connect to Related Maxims
This maxim is a specific application of the broader principle 'العادة محكمة' (Custom is authoritative). It also interacts with 'المعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً' (What is known by custom is like a stipulated condition).
Understanding these relationships strengthens your command of Hanafi legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نتيجة: الأحكام المبنية على العرف تتبدل بتبدله. ومن الواضح بعدما تقدم أن جميع ما بني من الأحكام على العرف يتبدل بتبدل العرف ويدور معه كيفما استدار. ذلك لأن...
Conclusion: Rulings based on custom change with its change. It is clear from what has preceded that all rulings that are built upon custom change with the change of custom and revolve with it wherever it turns. That is because...
Source pages p. 838

Commentary
This is a fundamental legal maxim in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: 'الأحكام المبنية على العرف تتبدل بتبدله' (Rulings based on custom change with its change). The author presents it as the conclusion of the discussion on custom. It means that when a ruling in fiqh is derived from or conditioned by a particular custom, that ruling is not static; if the custom evolves, the ruling may change accordingly. This applies particularly to areas like commercial transactions, documents, and procedures where custom plays a role. The reason ('ذلك لأن') is implicit: custom is the underlying 'illa (effective cause) for the ruling, so when the cause changes, the ruling follows. This maxim is a corollary of the larger maxim 'العادة محكمة' (Custom is authoritative). However, it is important to note that this only applies to rulings that are explicitly based on custom, not to fixed rulings derived from definitive texts (nusus).

Key Terms
العرف (al‑ʿurf): Custom that is consistent and accepted among people.
تبدل (tabaddul): Change or transformation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It remains unchanged to preserve legal stability.
B) It changes accordingly.
C) It becomes invalid until a new custom is established.
D) It becomes subject to the ruler's decree.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'الأحكام المبنية على العرف تتبدل بتبدله' means that fiqh rulings grounded in custom are dynamic, adapting to societal changes, but this does not affect rulings from definitive sacred texts.

The Maxim of Changing Rulings and the Authority of Custom
Explaining the maxim and its application to custom scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the maxim 'rulings change with times' and apply the distinction between general custom and specific custom in fatwa practice.

Core Idea
Rulings follow their causes; as times change, so do rulings — but specific custom stays local.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi principle: that legal rulings are not frozen in time but adapt to changing circumstances, especially through custom. The great maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' encapsulates this. However, custom is not monolithic — there is a vital distinction between general custom ('urf 'amm) and specific custom ('urf khas). A mufti must know which custom governs a case and respect its boundaries.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its basis
The maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' means that legal rulings can change with the passage of time, place, and conditions. This is because a ruling revolves with its 'illah (cause); when the cause changes, the ruling may change.
This is a major Hanafi qa'ida, codified in the Majalla (article 39).
2
Link the maxim to custom
Custom ('urf) is a primary vehicle through which this change occurs. Established customs influence rulings in mu'amalat and other areas, provided they do not conflict with a definitive text.
3
Distinguish general from specific custom
General custom ('urf 'amm) is widespread and applies universally. Specific custom ('urf khas) is confined to a particular place, time, or group. The passage stresses that specific custom's authority does not extend beyond its domain.
Example: The meaning of 'fard' in a locality may differ, and the local custom governs.
4
Apply the distinction in fatwa
When issuing a fatwa, first identify whether the relevant custom is general or specific. If specific, apply it only to those who practice it. If no custom exists, revert to general principles.
5
Recognize the limitation (istithna)
The maxim has an inherent exception: specific custom does not automatically become general. It remains tied to its context. A custom in one community does not bind another.
This is derived from the very logic of the maxim: change is contextual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٦٩) سلطان العرف

المقرر فقهاً أن الحكم يدور مع العلة .
وعن هذا وضعت القاعدة الفقهية القائلة: «لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان (ر) المجلة/ ٣٩ وشروحها). ولا فرق في العرف الذي تخضع له الأحكام بين العرف العام والعرف الخاص، غير أنَّ العرف الخاص بمكان أو بفئة من الناس يقتصر اعتباره وسلطانه على المكان أو الفئة اللذين استقرَّ العرف فيهما، ولا يتعداهما. فقد يكون العرف اللفظي أو العملي في موضوع ما ، كمعنى كلمة الفريضة الشرعية، وكتأجيل قدر من المهر أو تقسيط أجرة العقار، مختلفاً مکان بین وآخر، فيجري على أهل كل مكان حكم عرفهم الخاص. وسيأتي لذلك مزيد بیان (ر) شرح المجلة لعلي حيدر وللأتاسي م/ ٣٦ - ٤٠/ و الإحكام في تمييز الفتاوى عن الأحكام للشهاب القرافي المالكي في جواب السؤال ٣٩ ص ٦٨).
Chapter (69): The Authority of Custom. It is established in fiqh that the ruling revolves with the cause. And based on this, the legal maxim was placed: 'It is not denied that rulings change with the change of times' (al-Majalla/39 and its commentaries). And there is no difference in the custom to which rulings are subject between general custom and specific custom, except that specific custom to a place or a group of people is limited in its consideration and authority to the place or group in which the custom is established, and does not extend beyond them. For verbal or practical custom in a particular matter, like the meaning of the word 'fard' in Islamic law, or deferring part of the dowry or installment of rent for property, may differ from one place to another, so the ruling of their specific custom applies to the people of each place. More explanation will come. (See: Sharh al-Majalla by Ali Haidar and al-Atasi, articles 36-40; and al-Ihkam fi Tamyiz al-Fatawa an al-Ahkam by al-Shihab al-Qarafi al-Maliki, answer to question 39, p.68).
Source pages p. 839

Commentary
This passage is a legal maxim with its applications. It presents the major Hanafi maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' (Rulings change with changing times), which is one of the five universal maxims (al-qawa'id al-kubra) and is based on the principle that rulings depend on their causes. The author then applies this maxim to the concept of custom ('urf), distinguishing between general custom ('urf 'amm) and specific custom ('urf khas). General custom applies universally, while specific custom is limited to the particular place or group where it is established. Examples include the meaning of 'fard' (obligatory) in Islamic law or practices regarding dowry and rent. The passage also indicates that more details will follow, and it cites authoritative references.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom, whether verbal or practical, that is established among people.
سلطان العرف (sultan al-urf): The authority or binding power of custom in legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It always overrides general custom
D) It has no legal authority

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'la yunkar taghayyur al-ahkam bi taghayyur al-azman' empowers custom to shape rulings, but specific custom remains bounded to its time, place, and community.

Fourth Condition: Custom Must Not Invalidate a Decisive Text or Principle
Stating the fourth condition for valid custom: no violation of decisive evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the fourth condition for the validity of custom and apply it to distinguish between custom that is invalid and custom that is acceptable when conflicting with definitive evidence.

Core Idea
Custom conflicting with a definitive text is invalid; harmony preserves custom's validity.

In this lesson, we examine the crucial boundary between custom ('urf) and the primary sources of Shari'ah. The fourth condition for valid custom is that it must not lead to the suspension of a definitive text (nass qat'i) or a definitive principle (asl qat'i). This ensures that divine revelation takes precedence over human practice. However, when custom can be harmonized with the text without suspending it, it remains operative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the fourth condition: custom must not render a definitive text or principle inoperative.
2
Classify the evidence
Determine whether the conflicting evidence is decisive (qat'i) or speculative (zanni). Only qat'i texts and principles invalidate custom.
3
Apply the consequence
If the evidence is qat'i and custom suspends it, then custom is invalid. If the evidence is not qat'i or harmony is possible, custom remains valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۷۰/٥ - الشريطة الرابعة أن لا يكون في العرف تعطيل لنص ثابت، أو لأصل قطعي في الشريعة : إذا كان العرف مخالفاً لبعض الأدلة الشرعية من نصوص الشريعة أو من قواعدها وأصولها ، فالمبدأ العام الذي يُستخلص من أقوال الفقهاء الباحثين إجمالاً أنه : إذا ترتب على العمل بالعرف تعطيل لنص شرعي أو أصل قطعي في الشريعة لم يكن عندئذ للعرف اعتبار ، لأن نص الشارع مقدم على العرف . وأما إذا لم يترتب على العرف هذا التعطيل، بل كان العرف مما يمكن تنزيل النص الشرعي عليه أو التوفيق بينهما فالعرف عندئذ معتبر، وله سلطان محترم .. والتمييز بين هاتين الحالتين يقوم على نظر تفصيلي تختلف فيه النتائج بحسب كون العرف مخالفاً لنص أو أصل قطعي، أو مخالفاً لأدلة اجتهادية، وكذا بحسب كون العرف المخالف للنص مقارناً لوروده أو حادثاً بعده، وبحسب كون العرف لفظياً أو عملياً، وعاماً أو خاصاً. وهذا ما سنشرحه في الفصول (۷۱) - (٧٤) التالية التي نفردها للبحث في مخالفة العرف للأدلة الشرعية.
5/70 - The fourth condition: That custom does not render a fixed text or a definitive principle of Shari'ah inoperative. If custom contradicts some of the legal proofs from the texts of Shari'ah or its rules and principles, then the general principle derived from the statements of research jurists in summary is: If acting upon custom results in suspending a legal text or a definitive principle of Shari'ah, then custom has no weight, because the text of the Lawgiver takes precedence over custom. However, if this suspension does not result from the custom, but rather the custom is such that the legal text can be applied to it or harmony can be achieved between them, then custom is considered authoritative and has due respect. Distinguishing between these two cases requires detailed analysis, with results differing depending on whether the custom contradicts a text or definitive principle, or contradicts ijtihad-based evidence, and also depending on whether the custom contradicting the text was concurrent with its revelation or arose afterwards, and depending on whether the custom is verbal or practical, general or specific. This is what we will explain in the following chapters (71) to (74), which we dedicate to the study of custom's contradiction of legal proofs.
Source pages p. 845

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth condition for the validity of custom: it must not lead to the suspension (ta'til) of a definitive text (nass thabit) or a definitive principle (asl qat'i) of Shari'ah. The author states the general principle: if applying custom would suspend a definitive textual evidence, the custom is disregarded because the divine text is superior. However, if the custom can be harmonized with the text without suspending it, then the custom is valid and respected. The distinction between these two scenarios requires detailed examination based on several factors: whether the textual evidence is definitive or ijtihadi (speculative), whether the custom existed at the time of the revelation or arose later, and whether the custom is verbal or practical, general or specific. The author promises to elaborate on these distinctions in subsequent chapters (71-74). This condition highlights the supremacy of definitive Shari'ah sources over local customs, while allowing for custom to operate within the framework of Shari'ah when no clear conflict exists.

Key Terms
النص الثابت (al-nass al-thabit): A decisive textual evidence from Qur'an or Mutawatir Hadith that is clear and unambiguous
الأصل القطعي (al-asl al-qat'i): A definitive principle of Shari'ah derived from decisive proofs

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Custom is valid only if it does not suspend a definitive text or principle; harmony is permitted when the evidence is not decisive.

Custom Arising After a General Text Cannot Specify It
States the rule that later custom cannot specify a general text, with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that later custom cannot specify a general text and apply it to distinguish valid from invalid specification by custom.

Core Idea
A custom that arises after a text cannot specify it; that would be abrogation, not specification.

This lesson addresses a critical boundary in the theory of custom ('urf) as a source of law. While customs that existed at revelation can help specify general texts, customs that emerge later cannot change the meaning of a text already fixed. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a mufti to avoid invalid rulings.

Guided Path
1
Define 'al-urf al-hadith'
Identify what is meant by 'custom arising after the text': any practice or linguistic usage that appears after the revelation of a general text.
This contrasts with 'al-urf al-muqarin' (contemporaneous custom).
2
State the rule
State clearly: 'Custom arising after a general text is not valid to specify it' – this is the qawida from the passage, by consensus of the jurists.
The passage claims 'bi-ittifaq al-fuqaha' (by agreement of scholars).
3
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
Explain why: if later custom could specify, it would amount to naskh (abrogation) by custom, which is impermissible because only the Lawgiver can abrogate.
The passage warns this would destabilise the entire Sharia.
4
Distinguish from contemporaneous custom
Contrast with custom existing at the time of revelation: such custom is considered contextual evidence and can specify the text, as in the Maliki example of noble women hiring wet-nurses.
The rule is: only the custom at the time of text fixes the intended meaning.
5
Apply the rule
Apply to a scenario: e.g., a modern custom permits something the Quran forbids. The ruling is that the later custom is disregarded; the text stands.
Verbal custom changes are also invalid; only the original linguistic understanding matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
المراضع. قال ابن العربي: إن هذا تخصيص لعموم قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْوَالِدَاتُ يُرْضِعْنَ أَولَدَهُنَّ حَوْلَيْنِ كَامِلَيْنِ﴾. (ر) أحكام القرآن لابن العربي تحت الآية ۳۲۳ من سورة البقرة.
يعد بعض المالكية هذه المسألة من فروع تخصيص النص بالمصلحة، ولكن المصلحة التي يقصدونها هنا إنما هي مراعاة العرف الجاري في صَوْن النساء ذوات الشرف والنبل عن الإرضاع وتربية أولادهن بطريق الاسترضاع وهو من الأعراف التي لا تنافي قواعد الشريعة ونصوصها فتكون رعايته من المصالح، ويخصص به النص الشرعي . وقد كانت عادة العرب النبلاء في الجاهلية أن يسترضعوا لأولادهم مرضعات في البادية ابتغاء صحة الأجسام وتقويم اللسان.
الفصل الثالث والسبعون: العرف الحادث بعد النص العام المعارض له
إذا كان العرف المعارض للنص العام حادثاً بعد ذلك النص، فإنّ هذا العرف لا يعتبر ولا يصلح مخصصاً للنص التشريعي باتفاق الفقهاء، ولو كان عرفاً عاماً ; لأن العرف الحادث هو طارىء بعد أن حدد مفهوم النص التشريعي ومراد الشارع منه وأصبح نافذاً منذ صدوره عن الشارع . فإذا ساغ تخصيصه بعد ذلك بعرف طارىء مخالف له كان ذلك نسخاً للنص التشريعي بالعرف، وهذا غير جائز، إذ لو جاز لأدى إلى تبديل معظم أحكام الشريعة بأعراف طارئة تلغيها وتحل محلها، فلا يبقى للشرع معنى.
ولا فرق بين العرف العملي والعرف اللفظي في عدم صلوح الطارىء منهما لتخصيص النص السابق عليه بل العرف اللفظي الحادث هو أبعد عن الصلوح لهذا التخصيص.
فبعد ورود نصوص القرآن والسنة إذا تبدل عرف الناس في معاني بعض الألفاظ الواردة في نصوصهما فلا عبره لهذا التبدل، بل العبرة لتلك المعاني والحدود التي كانت هي المفهومة منهما عند الورود، لأن العرف اللفظي القائم عند ورود النص التشريعي هو الذي يحدد مراد الشارع من كلامه.
Chapter Nine: Theory of Custom. Breastfeeding. Ibn al-Arabi said: This is a specification of the general meaning of His statement: 'And mothers shall breastfeed their children for two complete years.' (Reference: Ahkam al-Quran by Ibn al-Arabi under verse 323 of Surah al-Baqara). Some Malikis consider this issue to be among the branches of specifying the text by public interest, but the interest they intend here is only taking into account the prevailing custom of protecting noble women from breastfeeding and raising their children by hiring wet nurses, which is among the customs that do not contradict the rules and texts of Sharia, so observing it is a benefit, and the legal text is specified by it. The noble Arabs in the pre-Islamic era used to hire wet nurses for their children in the desert for physical health and correct speech. Chapter Seventy-Three: Custom arising after a general text that opposes it. If a custom opposing a general text arises after that text, then this custom is not considered and is not valid as a specification for the legislative text, according to the agreement of the jurists, even if it is a general custom; because the arising custom is an occurrence after the concept of the legislative text and the intent of the Lawgiver from it have been determined and it became effective since its issuance from the Lawgiver. If its specification after that by an opposing arising custom were permissible, that would be abrogation of the legislative text by custom, and this is not permissible; because if it were allowed, it would lead to the replacement of most Sharia rulings by arising customs that abolish them and take their place, leaving no meaning for the Sharia. There is no difference between practical custom and verbal custom regarding the invalidity of the arising one to specify the preceding text; rather, the arising verbal custom is even more unsuitable for this specification. After the revelation of the texts of the Quran and Sunnah, if people's custom changes regarding the meanings of some words used in those texts, then this change is disregarded; rather, consideration is given to those meanings and limits that were understood from them at the time of revelation, because the verbal custom existing at the time of the legislative text is what determines the Lawgiver's intent from His speech.
Source pages p. 860

Commentary
This is a ruling on the legal weight of custom relative to general texts. The passage begins with an example from Malikis: they specify the general Quranic command for mothers to breastfeed by the custom of noble women hiring wet-nurses. This is valid because the custom existed before the text or contemporaneously, and it does not contradict Sharia. The author then presents the core rule: if a custom arises after a general text and contradicts it, it cannot specify that text, by consensus of all jurists. The reasoning is that the text already fixed its meaning and application at the time of revelation; allowing later custom to change that would be abrogation (naskh) by custom, which is forbidden as it would destabilise the entire Sharia. The rule applies equally to practical customs (like the breastfeeding example) and verbal customs (changes in word meanings). In fact, verbal customs arising after are even less acceptable because they would change the intended meaning of the Qur'an and Sunnah. The passage concludes by emphasising that when interpreting texts, only the verbal custom at the time of revelation matters; later linguistic shifts are irrelevant. This principle is fundamental for a mufti: he must not be swayed by modern customs that contradict explicit texts.

Key Terms
العرف (al-urf): Custom or prevailing practice that is established among people and does not contradict Sharia.
التخصيص (al-takhsis): Specification of a general term to some of its individuals, excluding others.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a ruling by a later evidence, which is only allowed from the Lawgiver, not by custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a custom arising after a general text invalid as a specifier?
A) Because custom is not a valid source of law in any case.
B) Because it would amount to abrogation of the text by custom, which is impermissible.
C) Because the text is too general to be specified by any custom.
D) Because only practical customs can specify texts, not verbal ones.

Key Takeaway
A later custom cannot specify a general text; only customs existing at the time of revelation can.

8 min
The Rule of Custom When It Conflicts with a General Text
Determining when a later custom may override a general textual ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Zarqa’s principle that a newly arising custom is valid only when it removes the effective cause (‘illa) of a general text, and distinguish this from Ibn ‘Abidin’s and Abu Sunna’s positions.

Core Idea
A later custom can only override a general text if it eliminates the text’s underlying cause.

This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Aam tackles a subtle usul problem: can a custom that arises after a general text specify it? The author presents three views—Ibn ‘Abidin’s, Abu Sunna’s, and his own resolution based on ‘illa. Understanding this distinction helps muftis navigate modern customs that appear to conflict with established rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core problem
Recognize the scenario: a custom emerges after a general text is revealed, and appears to contradict it. The question is whether this later custom can be given legal effect as a specifier (mukhassis) of the text.
This is different from pre-existing custom, which is widely accepted as a source.
2
State Ibn ‘Abidin’s position
Ibn ‘Abidin, drawing from al-Tahrir, holds that a general custom can specify a general text regardless of whether the custom preceded or postdated the text. He does not distinguish between old and new custom.
His view is based on the idea that widespread practice can function as evidence of specification.
3
Analyze Abu Sunna’s critique and alternative
Abu Sunna argues that Ibn ‘Abidin misapplied al-Tahrir; only pre-existing custom can specify. For new custom, he requires support from another text, consensus, or compelling necessity.
Al-Zarqa notes this alternative shifts the issue from custom vs. text to text vs. text and is hard to apply.
4
Understand al-Zarqa’s resolution via ‘illa
Al-Zarqa proposes that a newly arising custom is valid if it removes the effective cause (‘illa) of the general text. The text is already considered specified by its own cause; the custom indicates the cause is absent, so the ruling does not apply.
This is ‘specification by ‘illa’ (takhsis bi al-‘illa), not specification by custom directly.
5
Apply to a furu‘ case
Example: a vitiating condition in a contract. Normally such a condition invalidates the contract because of a specific ‘illa (e.g., gharar). If later custom makes such conditions widespread and the ‘illa no longer exists (e.g., the uncertainty is eliminated), the condition becomes valid and the contract stands.
The custom does not abrogate the text; it simply reveals that the text’s cause is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب التاسع : نظرية العرف
٢٨٤، وفتح القدير ١٧٥/٦ طبعة الميمنية) .

(۱) بحث العلامة المحقق ابن عابدین رحمه الله في حكم تعارض العرف والنص في رسالته «نشر العرف»، واستخلص من أقوال الفقهاء ضابطاً خلاصته أنه :
إذا خالف العرف النص من كل وجه بأن كان النص خاصاً في الموضوع بحيث يلزم من العمل بالعرف ترك النص فلا شك في رد العرف كتعارف الناس كثيراً من المحرمات الممنوعة بنصوص خاصة بها. وأما إذا لم يخالفه من كل وجه، كما لو كان النص عاماً والعرف يخالفه في بعض تطبيقاته فإنّ العرف حينئذ معتبر إذا كان عرفاً عاماً لأن العرف العام يصلح مخصصاً للنص . ولم يميز في هذا الضابط بين العرف القائم عند ورود النص، والحادث بعده واستند ابن عابدين في ذلك إلى نص نقله عن «التحرير» للكمال بن الهمام. ولكن الأستاذ الجليل أحمد فهمي أبو سنة في رسالته العرف والعادة في رأي الفقهاء» التي ظهرت خلال الطبعة الثالثة من كتابنا هذا قد ردّ ما حققه ابن عابدين في هذه القضية رداً ،وجيهاً وبيّن أن كلام صاحب التحرير هو في العرف القديم القائم عند ورود النص التشريعي أما العرف الطارىء فلا يصلح لتخصيص النص العام، ولو كان عرفاً عاماً.

۹۲۹
Chapter Nine: The Theory of Custom.
284, and Fath al-Qadir 6/175, Maymaniyya edition.
If people become accustomed to some practices that are legally reprehensible according to general legal texts, and they become commonly known in some seasons or funerals, such as the custom of mourning the dead in a manner exceeding the prescribed limits in duration or appearance, and such as men dancing with women at weddings and celebrations, which is considered legally as manifest transgression, and such as uncovering parts of the limits of the ‘awrah in some work or games, or they commonly practice usury in some situations or with some measures, then their habituation to these and similar practices is neither a justification nor proof; rather it must be prevented and suppressed because all prohibitions and wrongdoings are only prohibited by the Sharia due to the corruptions and evils they contain which ruin the family and society, and take social life away from the path of righteousness and perfection which the Sharia aims to guide towards. So people’s habituation to any of these legally abhorrent wrongdoings and corruptions does not remove the cause of their prohibition, because their prohibition was not based on their lack of habituation but on the evil of their consequences, and this is general and increases with habituation and does not decrease. (1)
From:
(1) The distinguished researcher Ibn ‘Abidin, may Allah have mercy on him, investigated the ruling on the conflict of custom and text in his treatise “Nashr al-‘Urf”, and derived from the statements of the jurists a principle whose summary is: If custom contradicts the text completely such that the text is specific to the matter and acting on the custom would necessitate abandoning the text, then there is no doubt that the custom is rejected, like people’s common practice of many prohibited things prohibited by specific texts. But if it does not contradict it completely, such as when the text is general and the custom contradicts it in some of its applications, then the custom is considered valid if it is a general custom, because a general custom can serve as a specifier (mukhassis) of the text. He did not distinguish in this principle between the custom existing at the time of the text’s revelation and the one arising afterward, and Ibn ‘Abidin relied on a text he transmitted from “al-Tahrir” by al-Kamal ibn al-Humam. However, the venerable professor Ahmad Fahmi Abu Sunna, in his treatise “al-‘Urf wa al-‘Adah fi Ra’y al-Fuqaha’” which appeared during the third edition of our book, refuted what Ibn ‘Abidin established in this issue, a sound refutation, and explained that the statement of the author of al-Tahrir concerns the old custom existing at the time of the legislative text’s revelation; as for the newly arising custom, it is not suitable for specifying a general text, even if it is a general custom.
928
Chapter (73): The Custom Arising After a General Text That Contradicts It
This is correct, but Professor Abu Sunna chose another basis whose summary is: The newly arising custom, if it has a legal basis from a text, consensus, or compelling necessity, is considered valid even if it contradicts a specific text; otherwise, it is not.
He elaborated on this aspect (see: Treatise on Custom and Habit in the Opinion of Jurists, p. 94).
...
What I see is that what Professor Abu Sunna held—though correct in itself—takes the issue out of its subject; because a custom contradicting some texts, if supported by another text, consensus, or a valid necessity according to the general Sharia texts negating hardship, then the issue is no longer of the type of conflict and specification between custom and text, but rather between one text and another text or consensus, as he himself pointed out, and this is contrary to the subject of the issue; moreover, this basis is difficult to control and verify when issues arise.
Therefore, I see that the correct principle in this matter is what we have verified here: that the newly arising custom, if it removes the ‘illa (effective cause) of the general text that apparently contradicts it, then it is considered; otherwise, it is not. And considering it then is not a matter of specifying a text by a newly arising custom, but rather of specification by the ‘illa, because the general text is then considered specified by a concomitant proof, namely its expressed or derived cause, and considering the newly arising custom that negates that cause is an application of the text after that specification.
We have seen evidence and proofs of this principle from the texts of our jurists in various issues: among them the issue of validating a vitiating contractual condition if custom has been practiced with it, and its juristic reasoning bears witness to this principle that we have verified.
This issue is indeed considered one of the most subtle juristic-usul points, and the most confusing for researchers.
929
Source pages p. 870

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-‘Aam discusses the interaction between custom (‘urf) and legal texts (nusus), specifically when a custom arises after a general text that apparently contradicts it. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by affirming that obviously sinful customs (like excessive mourning, mixed dancing, usury) are never justified by mere prevalence; their prohibition remains because it is based on inherent harm, not unfamiliarity.

He then presents Ibn ‘Abidin’s position from his treatise “Nashr al-‘Urf”: when custom contradicts a specific text, it is rejected; but when it contradicts a general text, a general custom can serve as a mukhassis (specifier) of that text, regardless of whether the custom existed before the text or arose later. Al-Zarqa notes that Ahmed Fahmi Abu Sunna refuted Ibn ‘Abidin, arguing that the view from al-Tahrir refers only to pre-existing custom; a later custom cannot specify a general text.

Al-Zarqa acknowledges Abu Sunna’s critique is correct but faults his alternative basis (custom is valid only if supported by another text, consensus, or necessity) because it shifts the issue from custom vs. text to text vs. text, and it is difficult to apply consistently.

Finally, al-Zarqa presents his own preferred principle: a newly arising custom is considered valid if it removes the effective cause (‘illa) of the general text. In that case, the custom does not ‘specify’ the text directly, but rather the text is understood as already specified by its own ‘illa; the custom merely indicates that the ‘illa is absent, so the ruling no longer applies. He provides the example of validating a vitiating contractual condition when custom supports it.

Key Terms
العرف الطارئ (al-‘urf al-tāri’): A custom that arises after the revelation of a legal text, not present at the time of legislation.
التخصيص بالعلة (al-takhṣīṣ bi al-‘illa): Specification of a general text by its own effective cause, meaning the text is understood as applying only where the cause exists.
العلة (al-‘illa): The effective cause or ratio legis behind a legal ruling; its presence triggers the ruling, its absence negates it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zarqa’s final position, when can a newly arising custom be considered valid despite contradicting a general text?
A) When it is a general custom accepted by the majority of people
B) When it is supported by another text, consensus, or necessity
C) When it removes the effective cause (illa) of the general text
D) When it does not contradict a specific text

Key Takeaway
A later custom can only override a general text if it removes the text’s underlying effective cause (‘illa), not merely by being widespread.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
8 min
Custom Overrides Analogical Rulings (الحكم القياسي يترك للعرف)
Statement of the rule that custom outweighs qiyas and istihsan in the absence of direct textual evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida that a ruling from qiyas is abandoned for custom, and identify when an exception applies.

Core Idea
If no text opposes, custom overrides qiyas – even a new custom – because custom indicates need.

In Hanafi usul al-iftah, when there is no direct text (Quran, Sunnah), jurists resort to qiyas (analogy) or istihsan (juristic preference). However, custom ('urf) often takes precedence over qiyas. This lesson examines a fundamental maxim that governs this hierarchy, with significant implications for fatwa in new contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of the ruling
Determine whether the ruling in question is based on a direct text (Quran, Sunnah) or on qiyas. If on qiyas, note the 'illa (effective cause) linking it to a textual case.
The maxim only applies when there is no text (specific or general) that directly addresses the issue.
2
Examine the existence of a custom
Check if there is a customary practice ('urf) relevant to the case. The custom can be pre-existing or new ('urf hadith).
The custom must be widespread and not contradictory to a clear text.
3
Apply the maxim: custom overrides qiyas
If the custom is not directly opposed by a text, the ruling from qiyas is abandoned in favor of the custom. This is based on the nearly unanimous agreement of Islamic ijtihads.
The Hanafi school considers this preference as a form of istihsan (juristic preference).
4
Check for exceptions
If the custom directly contradicts a specific or general text, the text prevails. The maxim is restricted by the principle that 'text takes precedence over custom'.
Also, custom cannot override a definitive text (qat'i).

Source Text

الفصل الرابع والسبعون

والاجتهادات الإسلامية تكاد تكون متفقة على أن الحكم القياسي يترك للعرف ولو كان عرفاً حادثاً، لأنَّ المفروض عندئذ أن هذا العرف لا يعارضه نص خاص ولا عام معارضة مباشرة والعرف غالباً دليل الحاجة، فهو أقوى من القياس، فيترجح عليه عند التعارض (ر: نشر العرف لابن عابدين في مجموعة رسائله ٢/ ١١٦).

۹۳۱
Chapter Seventy-Four: The case of conflict between custom and ijtihad. 1/74 - Firstly - Regarding ijtihad based on theoretical analogy (qiyas) and istihsan or istislah: the ijtihadi rulings that mujtahid jurists establish by deduction and derivation when there is no legal text on them: either they are established by way of theoretical analogy upon a ruling required by the Lawgiver by text due to the unity of cause between the analogized ruling and the analogized-from, or they are established by way of istihsan or istislah when there is no similar textually-provided ruling to analogize. Islamic ijtihads are almost unanimous that the ruling based on qiyas is abandoned for custom, even if it is a new custom, because then it is assumed that this custom is not directly opposed by a specific or general text, and custom is often an evidence of need, so it is stronger than analogy, and thus prevails over it when conflicting (see: Nashr al-'Urf by Ibn 'Abidin in his collection of treatises, 2/116). Rather, the verifying scholar Ibn al-Humam stated in Sharh al-Hidayah that 'custom has the status of consensus by law in the absence of a text' (see: Fath al-Qadir, 6/157). It is well-known that giving preference to custom over analogy is considered among the Hanafis and Malikis as a type of istihsan in which analogical evidences are abandoned for other evidences, including custom. And if custom prevails over analogy that is based on a legislative text... 931
Source pages p. 872

Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule regarding the hierarchy of sources when no direct text exists. The author first describes the types of ijtihad: qiyas (analogy) based on a textual ruling, and istihsan/istislah (juristic preference/public interest) when no analogy is possible. The main rule is that Islamic jurists are almost unanimous that a ruling derived from qiyas is abandoned in favor of custom, even if the custom is new, because it is assumed that such custom is not contradicted by any direct text, and custom often indicates necessity, making it stronger than mere analogy. This is supported by references: Ibn 'Abidin's 'Nashr al-'Urf' and Ibn al-Humam's 'Fath al-Qadir' where he equates custom to consensus in the absence of text. The Hanafi and Maliki schools consider this preference of custom over qiyas as a form of istihsan, where analogical evidences yield to other evidences like custom. This rule is fundamental in fatwa issuance: when no explicit text governs, custom takes precedence over analogical reasoning.

Key Terms
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Independent juristic reasoning to derive rulings in the absence of a direct text.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical deduction extending a ruling from a textual case to a new case due to shared 'illa.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that abandons qiyas for a stronger evidence like custom or necessity.
عرف (urf): Customary practice considered as evidence in the absence of text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the priority between a ruling derived from qiyas and a custom that appears after the qiyas?
A) The qiyas ruling always prevails because it is based on a text.
B) The custom prevails because it reflects need and is not contradicted by a direct text.
C) The earlier ruling prevails, whether qiyas or custom.
D) The opinion of the majority of scholars decides.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa methodology, when no text exists, custom prevails over qiyas, even if the custom is new, because custom reflects prevailing need and is akin to consensus in its evidentiary strength.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

When Custom Overrides Qiyas: The Hanafi Principle and Its Application
Explains how custom can override qiyas and how change in custom changes rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-ādah muḥakkamah' to determine when a custom overrides qiyas and how a change in custom alters the ruling when the illa is custom-based.

Core Idea
Custom overrides qiyas when no text conflicts; change in custom changes rulings when the illa is built on custom.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa explains how custom ('urf) can override qiyas and how rulings change with custom when their underlying cause ('illa) is custom-based. We examine two concrete examples: the father receiving his daughter's dower, and the usurper dyeing a garment black. The Hanafi school's handling of these cases illustrates the principle that custom is authoritative (al-ādah muḥakkamah) and that following changed custom is not abandoning qiyas but applying it consistently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qiyas rule
What does qiyas dictate in the default case? For the mahr example: a debt can only be paid to the creditor or his agent. For the dyeing example: any change to the surface of a usurped item is a defect if it reduces value.
2
Spot the custom
What custom conflicts with the qiyas result? In the mahr example, custom dictates that the father receives the dower. In the dyeing example, custom changes from black being a defect to being an increase.
3
Apply the maxim
How does 'al-ādah muḥakkamah' resolve the conflict? The custom is widespread, consistent, and does not contradict a clear text, so it overrides the qiyas result.
4
Trace the custom-based illa
In the dyeing example, the cause ('illa) for considering dyeing a defect or increase is based on whether the color is desired in custom. When custom changes, the 'illa changes, and the ruling follows.
5
Conclude the muftā bihi
Which position is operative after custom changes, and why? The Sahibayn's view (dyeing is an increase) became mufta bihi because custom changed; if Abu Hanifa were alive, he would agree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٧٤) تعارض العرف والاجتهاد
٣ - أن القواعد القياسية تقضي بأنه لا يجوز دفع الدين لغير صاحبه، ولا ينفذ قبضه على الدائن ما لم يكن للقابض نيابة عن الدائن من ولاية أو وكالة .
ولكن الفقهاء تركوا القياس في البنت البكر البالغة إذا قبض أبوها، أو جدها عند عدم الأب، مهرها من زوجها حين زواجها، واعتبروا هذا القبض نافذاً عليها ومبرئاً لذمة الزوج للعرف والعادة، ما لم يصدر منها نهي عن دفع المهر إلى سواها.
هذا إلى فروع كثيرة ترك الفقهاء فيها حكم القياس للعرف المخالف.
٣/٧٤ - ثانياً : في القياس المستند إلى علة عرفية: وإذا كان هذا سلطان العرف في الفقه تجاه القياس المستند إلى الشبه الذاتي في العلل بين الأحكام المنصوصة والأحكام المقيسة، فإنه يدلنا بطريق الأولوية على تبدل الأحكام القياسية بتبدل العرف عندما تكون علة القياس نفسها مبنية على العرف فعندئذ يكون هذا التبدل في الحكم تبعاً لتبدل العرف ليس خروجاً على القياس بل تمشياً معه .
١ - ففي المذهب الحنفي كان رأي إمامه أبي حنيفة أن غاصب الثوب إذا صَبَغَه أسود يعتبر ذلك تعييباً له، فيخير المالك أن بین يتركه له فيضمنه قيمته كما غصبه غير مصبوغ أو أن يأخذ الثوب مع تضمين الغاصب نقصان قيمته بهذا الصبغ وفقاً للحكم العام القياسي في تعيب المغصوب عند الغاصب.
فلما تبدل عرف الناس في اللون الأسود وأصبح مرغوباً فيه لما اتخذه العباسيون شعاراً قال الصاحبان بأن صبغ الغاصب الثوب بالأسود يعتبر زيادة فيه لا تعييباً له فيطبق عليه حكم زيادة الغاصب في المغصوب، فيخير المالك بين تركه له وتضمينه قيمته غير مصبوغ، أو أن يأخذه ويدفع للغاصب قيمة الزيادة صيانة لحقيهما. وقد نص فقهاء المذهب على أن هذا الاختلاف عن أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه في هذه المسألة وكثير من أمثالها اختلاف عرف وزمان لا اختلاف نظر ،وبرهان، فلو كان أبو حنيفة حياً لقال بما قال صاحباه عند تبدل العرف (ر: كتاب الغصب في «الكفاية» شرح الهداية ،۲۷۱/۸ ، وفي رد المحتار (١٢٥/٥ .
إنما هو خلاف
٢ - وفي المذاهب الأخرى ذكر القرافي المالكي في كتابه «الإحكام» أن الإفتاء بالأحكام التي مستندها العوائد بعد تغير تلك العوائد هو الإجماع، ووصف ذلك بأنه: «جهالة في الدين ممن يقولون: «إننا مقلدون ليس لنا إلا أن نفتي بما في الكتب من الأحكام المنقولة عن المجتهدين وقد بين أن تغير تلك الأحكام وفقاً للعوائد المتجددة ليس إنشاء اجتهاد ينقض اجتهاد المجتهدين، بل هو تطبيق لقاعدة أجمع عليها المجتهدون في الشريعة وأوجبوا السير في ظلها (۱) .
(۱) ر: «الإحكام للقرافي السؤال ٣٩ ص ٦٨ ، ونشر العرف لابن عابدين في رسائله ۱۲۵/۲، ورسالة العرف والعادة للأستاذ أبي سنة ص ١٠٥.
Chapter (74): Conflict between custom and ijtihad. 3 - Qiyas rules dictate that it is not permissible to pay a debt to someone other than its owner, and the creditor is not bound by the receipt unless the recipient has authority or agency from the creditor. However, the jurists abandoned qiyas in the case of an adult virgin daughter when her father, or her grandfather in the absence of the father, receives her dower from her husband at the time of marriage, and they considered this receipt as binding on her and discharging the husband's liability due to custom and habit, unless she had prohibited paying the dower to anyone else. This is among many branches where jurists abandoned the ruling of qiyas for opposing custom. 3/74 - Secondly: Concerning qiyas based on a customary cause: If this is the authority of custom in fiqh towards qiyas based on essential similarity in the causes between prescribed rulings and analogized rulings, then it indicates a fortiori that analogical rulings change with the change of custom when the cause of qiyas itself is built upon custom. In that case, this change in the ruling following the change of custom is not a departure from qiyas but rather in accordance with it. 1 - In the Hanafi school, the opinion of its imam Abu Hanifa was that if a usurper dyes a garment black, that is considered a defect, so the owner chooses either to leave it with him and make him liable for its value as usurped, undyed, or to take the garment while making the usurper liable for the decrease in its value due to this dye, according to the general analogical ruling regarding defective usurped property. Then when people's custom regarding the color black changed and it became desirable, because the Abbasids adopted it as their emblem, the Two Companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said that dyeing black is considered an increase, not a defect, so the ruling for increase by the usurper applies: the owner chooses either to leave it and make him liable for its value undyed, or to take it and pay the usurper the value of the increase, preserving both rights. The jurists of the school stated that this difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions in this issue and many similar ones is a difference of custom and time, not a difference of opinion and proof; if Abu Hanifa were alive, he would say what his two companions said when custom changed. (Ref: Kitab al-Ghasb in al-Kifayah, Sharh al-Hidayah, 8/271, and Radd al-Muhtar, 5/125). This is merely a difference. 2 - In other schools, al-Qarafi al-Maliki mentioned in his book al-Ihkam that issuing fatwas based on rulings whose basis is customs after those customs have changed is a matter of consensus, and he described that as 'ignorance of religion on the part of those who say: we are imitators, we have nothing but to issue fatwas according to what is in the books of rulings transmitted from the mujtahids.' He explained that changing those rulings according to renewed customs is not creating ijtihad that overturns the ijtihad of the mujtahids, but rather an application of a principle upon which the mujtahids have agreed in the Sharia and obligated proceeding under its shadow. (1) (1) See: al-Ihkam by al-Qarafi, Question 39, p. 68; Nashr al-Urf by Ibn Abidin, 2/125; and Risalah al-Urf wa al-Adah by Prof. Abu Sinnah, p. 105.
Source pages p. 874

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between custom ('urf) and qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Islamic jurisprudence, specifically within the Hanafi school. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, presents two main aspects: first, cases where custom overrides qiyas directly; second, cases where the 'illa (ratio legis) of qiyas itself is based on custom, so that changing custom leads to a change in the ruling while still adhering to qiyas.

The first example is from the chapter on marriage: normally, a debt can only be paid to the creditor or his agent. However, custom dictates that a father (or grandfather) often receives his adult virgin daughter's mahr (dower). The jurists set aside the strict qiyas and accept this custom, deeming the receipt valid unless the daughter objects. This illustrates how custom can modify a general rule.

The second example is more nuanced: the case of a usurper who dyes a stolen garment black. Abu Hanifa, based on the custom of his time (where black dye was considered a defect), ruled that the owner could either take the garment and claim compensation for the defect, or leave it and demand full value. Later, when the Abbasids made black a popular color, the custom changed, and people began to desire black garments. Consequently, the Two Companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) revised the ruling, deeming the dyeing an increase (not a defect). The owner now either leaves the garment and gets full value, or takes it and pays the usurper the added value. The Hanafi jurists emphasize that this is a difference of custom and time, not an error in ijtihad. If Abu Hanifa were alive, he would agree with his companions due to the changed custom.

The author then cites al-Qarafi (Maliki) to show that other schools also recognize the necessity of updating fatwas when customs change. Al-Qarafi states that those who blindly follow old rulings despite changed customs are ignorant, because adapting rulings to new customs is not a rejection of the mujtahids but an application of a consensus-based principle.

The key takeaway is that custom has a powerful role in shaping fiqh, especially when the underlying rationale of a ruling is custom-based. The Hanafi school's position is that this change is not a violation of qiyas but its consistent application.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Custom or habitual practice that is widespread and consistent among people and does not contradict a clear text.
علة (illa): The effective cause or ratio legis for a ruling, upon which qiyas is based.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning applied to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared 'illa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of the usurper dyeing a garment black, why did the Sahibayn differ from Abu Hanifa?
A) They made a mistake in qiyas.
B) Custom regarding black color changed over time.
C) They did not consider custom at all.
D) Abu Hanifa retracted his opinion.

Key Takeaway
Custom can override qiyas when it is widespread and does not contradict a clear text; when the illa itself is custom-based, a change in custom changes the ruling in line with qiyas.

Custom and Change of Time: The Maxim of Temporal Change
Introducing the maxim that rulings change with time and its rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' to identify when an ijtihad-based ruling should change and distinguish it from fixed textual rulings.

Core Idea
Ijtihad-based rulings change with time; fixed texts remain unchanged.

The Hanafi legal tradition recognizes that temporal conditions affect ijtihad-based rulings. Later jurists often revised fatwas to maintain justice as circumstances evolved. This lesson introduces the foundational maxim from the Majalla that legitimizes such change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of Sharia rulings
Recognize that rulings aim to establish justice and prevent harm, and are closely tied to context.
2
Understand the problem of temporal change
A ruling effective in one era may become ineffective or counterproductive due to changed conditions, means, or morals.
3
Analyze the response of later jurists
Later jurists from all schools issued fatwas contrary to earlier imams, citing change of time and corruption of morals as the reason.
They believed early imams would have done the same if they lived later.
4
State the maxim and its source
The maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' is recorded in the Majalla (Article 39). It is the basis for adapting rulings.
The maxim is attributed to Ibn Abidin's Nashr al-Urf.
5
Distinguish changeable from unchangeable rulings
Only ijtihad-based rulings (especially in mu'amalat) are subject to change; fixed textual rulings (ibadat) remain constant.
The passage implies this distinction, though the text is cut short.

العرف وتغير الزمان : نظرة عامة

وعن هذا أفتى الفقهاء المتأخرون من شتى المذاهب الفقهية في كثير من المسائل بعكس ما أفتى به أئمة مذاهبهم وفقهاؤها الأولون، وصرّح هؤلاء المتأخرون بأن سبب اختلاف فتواهم عمن سبقهم هو اختلاف الزمان وفساد الأخلاق. فليسوا في الحقيقة مخالفين للسابقين من فقهاء مذاهبهم، بل لو وجد الأئمة الأولون في عصر المتأخرين، ورأوا اختلاف الزمان والأخلاق لعدلوا إلى ما قال المتأخرون.

وقد اتفقت كلمة فقهاء المذاهب على أن الأحكام التي تتبدل بتبدل
Chapter Seventy-Six: Custom and Change of Time: General Overview. 1/76 - It is established in the fiqh of the Sharia that changing circumstances and temporal conditions have a great effect on many ijtihad-based Sharia rulings. These rulings are an organization required by the Sharia aiming to establish justice, bring benefits, and repel harms. They are closely connected with temporal conditions, means, and public morals. Many a ruling was an effective measure and remedy for an environment in a specific time, but after a generation or generations it no longer achieves its purpose, or even leads to the opposite, due to changes in conditions, means, and morals. Because of this, later jurists from various schools of fiqh issued fatwas in many cases contrary to what the founders of their schools and earlier jurists had issued, and these later jurists stated that the reason for their differing fatwas from their predecessors was the change of time and corruption of morals. They are not truly opposing the earlier jurists of their schools; rather, if the early founders were in the age of the later jurists and saw the change of time and morals, they would have adopted what the later jurists said. (See: Nashr al-Urf by Ibn Abidin in his Collection of Treatises 2/125). On this basis the legal maxim was founded: 'It is not denied that rulings change with the changing of times' (M/39). 2/76 - Which are the rulings that must change with the change of time: And the consensus of jurists of the schools is that the rulings that change with change...
Source pages p. 881

Commentary
This passage introduces the important usul al-iftai principle that rulings change with changing times. The author begins by noting that Sharia rulings derived through ijtihad are closely tied to their temporal context, as they aim to achieve justice and public interest. When conditions change, a ruling that was once effective may become counterproductive. Consequently, later jurists from all schools often issued fatwas opposite to those of earlier imams, explicitly attributing this to changed times and morals. These jurists argued that the earlier founders would have done the same if they lived in the later period. The author cites Ibn Abidin's Nashr al-Urf as a source and then presents the well-known legal maxim from the Majalla: 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' (It is not denied that rulings change with the changing of times) (Article 39). The passage then begins to address which rulings are subject to change, but the text is cut off. The core idea is that ijtihad-based rulings are dynamic and must adapt to prevent injustice, while fixed texts remain unchanged.

Key Terms
العرف (al-'urf): Custom; prevailing practice in a society that is not contrary to Sharia.
تغير الزمان (taghayyur al-zaman): Change of time or temporal conditions that affect the context of legal rulings.
القاعدة الفقهية (al-qa'idah al-fiqhiyyah): A legal maxim; a general principle from which many specific rulings are derived.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) They followed a different madhhab.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'لا ينكر تغير الأحكام بتغير الأزمان' allows ijtihad-based rulings to adapt to preserve justice, while fixed textual rulings remain unchanged.

Change of Ijtihadi Rulings Due to Corruption of Time
Explanation of the principle and two Hanafi applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Ijtihadi rulings change with corruption of time' to identify when a fatwa should be modified in light of new circumstances.

Core Idea
When public morality or conditions change, ijtihadi rulings must adapt to avoid harm or futility.

This passage introduces a key Hanafi maxim that allows later jurists to revise earlier ijtihadi rulings when circumstances render them harmful or futile. The author provides two concrete examples from Hanafi fiqh: the debtor's fraudulent property transfers and the usurper's liability for usufruct. Understanding this principle is essential for a mufti to issue relevant fatwas without violating Shari'ah fundamentals.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the qawida: 'تغير الأحكام الاجتهادية بفساد الزمان'. Note that it applies to ijtihadi rulings, not fixed textual rulings. Understand the two types of 'corruption of time': moral decay and new organizational/economic conditions.
2
Understand the rationale
The rationale is that Shari'ah is free from harm and futility. If a ruling becomes harmful or futile due to changed circumstances, it must be revisited to maintain justice.
3
First furu' case: Debtor's dispositions
Trace the original ruling: a debtor's gifts and endowments are effective even if debts exceed assets. Identify the change: corruption of people's morals leading to fraudulent asset transfers. Then the new ruling: dispositions are valid only for surplus after debt.
4
Second furu' case: Usurper's liability
The original ruling: a usurper does not guarantee usufruct. The passage begins to explain but cuts off. Note how the pattern of original ruling → changed circumstance → new ruling is expected to apply.
This example is incomplete; caution needed in analysis.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference for iftaa: earlier jurists would uphold the original ruling, while later muftis apply the modified ruling. The mufti must assess whether 'corruption of time' applies to the case at hand.

From Chapter (76): Custom and Change of Time: An Overview. Corruption of morals, loss of piety, and weakness of restraint are what they call: corruption of time. And it may arise from the occurrence of new organizational situations and temporal means, such as legal orders, beneficial administrative arrangements, economic methods, and the like. This second type is also, like the first, a cause for changing the established ijtihadi fiqh rulings that were decided before it, if they become incompatible with it, because they then become futile or harmful, and the Shari'ah is free from that. We have previously quoted from al-Muwafaqat of al-Shatibi that there is no futility in the Shari'ah (see: F 2/64). We will now show examples of change of rulings due to change of times in both types. Chapter Seventy-Seven: Change of Ijtihadi Rulings Due to Corruption of Time. 1/77 - Among the issues in which later jurists changed the rulings established by the ijtihad of the early imams, attributing that to corruption of time, i.e., corruption of public morals, are the following cases: 2/77 - A - It is established in the original Hanafi school that a debtor's dispositions in his property by gift, endowment, and all forms of donation are effective, even if his debts consume all his property, because debts are attached to his liability (dhimma), so the physical assets of his property remain free, thus his disposition in them is effective. This is what the analogical rules require. Then, when people's consciences became corrupt, greed increased, piety decreased, and debtors took to smuggling their assets away from creditors by endowing or gifting them to relatives or friends they trust, the later jurists of the Hanbali and Hanafi schools gave fatwas that these dispositions of a debtor are not effective except for what exceeds the amount of debt from his property, as detailed in its place in the theory of legal capacity. (0/70) 3/77 - B - In the original Hanafi school, the usurper (ghasib) does not guarantee the value of the usufruct of the usurped property for the period of usurpation, but rather guarantees only the physical asset if it perishes or is damaged, because usufructs in their view are not property values in themselves, but rather become property values only through a contract.
Source pages p. 883

Commentary
This passage presents the legal maxim that ijtihadi rulings can change with the corruption of time (فساد الزمان). The author distinguishes two types of 'corruption of time': moral decay and new organizational/economic conditions. Both necessitate revisiting earlier rulings if they become harmful or futile. The text then provides two applications from Hanafi fiqh. First (A): The original Hanafi rule allowed a debtor to give away all his property even if it left creditors unpaid, because debts are personal liabilities. But when moral corruption led debtors to fraudulently transfer assets, later Hanafi (and Hanbali) jurists restricted this rule, making dispositions invalid except for surplus after debt. Second (B): The original Hanafi rule held that a usurper (ghasib) does not compensate for usufruct (usufruct not being a property right), but this passage introduces the issue but cuts off before the modern modification. The author follows the sequence: first stating the original ruling, then identifying the changed circumstance, then presenting the new ruling. This exemplifies the principle that rulings are not static but respond to social reality, as long as change is grounded in the Shari'ah's objectives.

Key Terms
فساد الزمان (fasad al-zaman): Corruption of time: moral decay or new circumstances that render existing rulings inappropriate.
اجتهاد (ijtihad): Strictly reasoning of jurists to derive rulings from sources, not speculative opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did later Hanafi jurists change the ruling on a debtor's dispositions?
A) Because the original ruling was wrong.
B) Due to corruption of people's morals and common fraud.
C) Because of a new Quranic verse.
D) Due to a difference between Abu Hanifa and his students.

Key Takeaway
Ijtihadi rulings are dynamic and can change with societal conditions, but always within the framework of Shari'ah objectives.

Change of Ijtihadic Rulings Due to Evolving Means: 'Iddah in Judicial Divorce
Explains how new judicial systems affect the start of 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that ijtihadic rulings change with evolving means to determine the start of 'iddah in modern judicial systems with appeal processes.

Core Idea
A ruling based on a particular means changes when the means evolve; the 'iddah begins only upon finalization of a divorce ruling, not the initial decree.

Mustafa al-Zarqa illustrates how the qawida 'taghayyur al-ahkam bi-taghayyur al-zaman wa al-ahwal' applies to the modern judiciary. He shows that the 'iddah start must be re-evaluated given the possibility of appeal, aligning with the maqasid of preserving lineage and avoiding confusion. This lesson walks through the reasoning and its practical implications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing qawida
The passage exemplifies the Hanafi principle: 'Ijtihadic rulings change with the evolution of means.' Understand that when the underlying means (e.g., judicial finality) change, the ruling derived from those means must be re-evaluated.
This is a sub-maxim of the broader qawida: 'It is not denied that rulings change with changing times and circumstances.'
2
Recognize the classical ruling
In classical Hanafi fiqh, a judge's ruling was final and immediately enforceable. Therefore, the 'iddah for a judicially divorced woman began immediately upon the ruling, because the marriage was considered terminated at that moment.
Classical judiciary was single-tier; no appeals existed.
3
Identify the changed means
Modern judicial systems introduce appeals and cassation, meaning a ruling is no longer final at the moment it is issued. The means of enforcement has changed from immediate finality to a suspended state pending appeal.
This new system is based on public interest (masalih mursala) and is not contrary to Sharia.
4
Apply the qawida to derive the new ruling
Since the finality of the ruling (the relevant 'illah) has changed, the ruling on the start of 'iddah must change. The 'iddah now begins only when the ruling becomes final (mubram), not from the initial decree. This avoids legal impossibility if the divorce is overturned on appeal after the woman has remarried.
The initial ruling is treated as suspended (ta'liq) pending finalization.
5
Confirm via analogy with revocable divorce
The author draws a parallel to talaq raj'i, where the marriage continues despite the pronouncement during the 'iddah period. Similarly, before finalization of the judicial divorce, the marriage is considered intact, preserving rights and obligations.
This strengthens the new ruling by showing it aligns with established Sharia principles.
